## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-26 19:59 UTC

I've now traced all the weapon parameters through to where they actually affect motion, and found something important. Here are precise answers.

## Your weapon-parameter questions

**`energy` vs `concentration` — they're redundant, and `energy` is the vestigial one.**
- `concentration` drives **ignition**: `σ = J/√cellArea · concentration`. It decides *whether* a crack starts. This is the real damage lever for the player.
- `energy` does **nothing** for the player anymore — player damage is `BulletMass × v × concentration`. It survives in only two places: scaling **alien** bullet mass relative to the reference player weapon (`bulletMass *= storedEnergy / refWc.Energy`, [FractureGameplay.cs:180](GameEngine/Gameplay/FractureGameplay.cs#L180)), and splitting shrapnel energy. So it's a damage backdoor for non-player sources, fully replaceable by giving aliens/shrapnel a real mass or concentration. **Drop it.**

**The three motion knobs — and the kicker:**
- **`momentumTransfer`** → body **recoil/knockback**. `kick = dir · (bulletSpeed · momentumTransfer)`, applied to the whole struck body at impact ([FractureService.cs:189](GameEngine/Engine/Destruction/FractureService.cs#L189)); fragments inherit it via `BodyLinear` (not re-added).
- **`ejectFraction`** → fragment **fly-apart speed**. `ejectSpeed = bulletSpeed · ejectFraction`; each fragment scatters radially from the impact point at `ejectSpeed · boost · rand` ([FractureSimulator.cs:535](GameEngine/Engine/Destruction/FractureSimulator.cs#L535)).
- **`impactSpin`** → fragment **tumble**. `angular = parent + impactSpin · shear`, where `shear = cross(spreadDir, shotDir)` — off-axis fragments spin consistently per side ([FractureSimulator.cs:544](GameEngine/Engine/Destruction/FractureSimulator.cs#L544)).

**The kicker:** the kinetic energy budget — `eKinetic = (1 − SurfaceFraction)·E` — is computed in `ComputeBudget`, stored in `FractureResult.EnergyKinetic`, and then **never used for motion**. Fragment fling is driven *entirely* by `ejectFraction × bulletSpeed`. So your energy model already computes a "kinetic share" and throws it away; the actual motion is a parallel bullet-speed heuristic. That's the same disease as the vanishing-brittleness-energy: a computed channel that doesn't connect to anything.

**This is exactly what unifies.** In the conservative model, the kinetic channel *becomes* the fling — fragment translation + spin are **derived** from the energy that actually reaches each fragment. That collapses `ejectFraction`, `impactSpin`, and `energy` into the energy accounting. What's genuinely separate is `momentumTransfer` (whole-body knockback is a gameplay-feel thing, not fragment physics) — that's worth keeping as one scalar. So weapon params go from **7 → ~4**: `concentration`, `directionality`, `blastFraction`, `knockback`.

Now two decisions before I write the plan.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-26 20:02 UTC

Both locked in: aggressive unification + strict conservation. Let me write the plan.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-26 20:03 UTC

The plan is written. Here's the shape of it.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-26 20:21 UTC

Good refinements — several of these cut even more parameters. Let me verify a couple of facts before I fold them into the plan.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-26 20:22 UTC

Verified both: **zero callers** of `TryFracture`/`Simulate` outside the engine, and `DetachOnSplit` is `true` in config and by default everywhere. Both safe to remove. Let me answer your other questions.

**One-frame fracture & DetachOnSplit** — confirmed dead/always-on. Remove the single-frame path entirely (`TryFracture`, `Simulate`, the standalone `Propagate`) and hardcode detach-on-split, dropping the flag from `FractureTiming`/`FractureProcess`/config/JSON.

**CrackSteps/CrackFrames → per-material.** Agreed, and I'd go further: collapse the *two* global pacing knobs into **one per-material `crackSpeed`** (cells/sec) that derives the steps/frames internally. Two pacing parameters is exactly the sprawl we're cutting, and a body has a single material so there's no ambiguity.

**Directionality edge cases** (these fall out of the conservative split cleanly):
- *No outward bonds are aligned* (crack hits a wall and must turn): with normalized weights, when every neighbor has `align ≤ 0` they all collapse to the same weight `(1−dir)`, so the transmit pool splits **equally** among them — the crack spreads/wraps sideways, energy conserved, nothing lost. There's an intuitive *alternative* worth feeling: convert the forward-blocked energy to **local dump** (fling/vaporize) so a focused shot that *can't* tunnel punches a crater instead of smearing sideways.
- *No intact bonds left* (isolated/last cell): the transmit pool has nowhere to go, so it becomes that cell's **fling** — a lone cell ejected with the leftover KE (or vaporizes if it clears the threshold). Same rule drains any energy left in the frontier when the graph/budget is exhausted. Conservative endpoint, no sink.

**BlastResist vs DensityMult** — you're right to be suspicious. In the new model the vaporize threshold ∝ cell **mass** = `Area · DensityMult`, so a dense cell *already* resists vaporization. `BlastResist` would be a second multiplier on that same threshold — on the vaporize axis it's **redundant** with DensityMult. They differ only in side-effects: DensityMult also makes the cell *heavier* (slower fling, more inertia, bigger mass share), whereas BlastResist resists pulverization *without* adding mass — i.e. light "ablative/heat-shield" armor. My recommendation: **drop it** (it's dead anyway), make mass the single resistance axis ("armor = dense"), and re-introduce a decoupled knob later only if you specifically want light-but-ablative material. Two questions to lock the model:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-26 20:46 UTC

Phase 1 is done. The prototype is at [prototypes/fracture-cells-v4.html](prototypes/fracture-cells-v4.html) — open it in a browser (no build needed).

## What it implements

The strictly-conservative channel model we designed. Every shot's energy is partitioned into **break / vaporize / fling / transmit**, shown as a live bar that sums to 100%:
- **Brittleness** is now a real partition — `dump = (1−brit)·e` stays local (crater + fling), `transmit = brit·e` travels as cracks. Low = blunt crater + big fling; high = long thin cracks.
- **Blast fraction** splits the local dump between vaporizing cells and flinging them.
- **Bonds accumulate stress** against a fixed strength; **re-clicking the same spot** accumulates until it cracks (sustained fire). Spin ω is a runtime multiplier on delivered *stress*, not a baked-in strength change.
- **Fling speed + tumble are derived** from the fling energy that actually reached each fragment — no ejectFraction/impactSpin knobs.
- **Vaporize threshold ∝ cell mass** (no BlastResist).
- **Blocked-crack toggle** (spread-sideways ↔ crater-dump) to feel both before the C# port.

Sliders re-preview a single fresh shot; clicks accumulate.

## Verification I ran (headless, against the real generated graph)

- **Conservation exact**: `break + vaporize + fling = E` to 0.0000% error across 360 trials (all brittleness × directionality × blast combos).
- **Brittleness → reach**: 0 → 0 broken bonds, monotonic up to 15 at brittleness 1.
- **Accumulation**: weak repeated shots crack from shot 3 onward where shots 1–2 did nothing.

Things to feel for when you play with it: sweep brittleness and watch the channel bar shift between fling and break; shoot the **dumbbell neck** at low energy; lower energy and tap the same spot to feel stress build; flip the blocked-crack mode with high directionality against the **crater** shape's concave corner.

Phase 2 (the C# port) is deferred per the plan — I'll wait until the prototype feels right to you before touching the engine. Tell me what to adjust in the feel and I'll iterate on the prototype.

I noticed a strange pattern, the cells closest to the impact don't vaporise ever with brittleness at 100, even at 100 blast fraction. Also, I'm thinking that when a cell vaporises, the surplus local energy is lost. So a 0 brittleness asteroid, when hit, just loses one cell no matter how big the impact was. How do you think we should handle this case? Make Channel the surplus energy as fling to the remaining fragment?

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-26 21:08 UTC

Both observations are correct, and they point at the same structural flaw. Let me confirm the mechanism in the code before proposing the fix.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-26 21:08 UTC

Confirmed — both bugs share one root. At line 521, when a cell vaporizes, the surplus (`vE - thr`) is added to `cells[i].flingE`, but that cell is then excluded from every fragment in `rebuildComponents`, so its fling energy rides a dust mote that just fades — effectively lost. And vaporization is gated by `dump = (1-brit)·e`, so at brittleness 1 there's no dump → no vaporize, ever.

Your instinct is right, and I'd generalize it: rather than dumping surplus onto the dying cell (or even onto "the remaining fragment" as a lump), let the **vaporization spill outward to intact neighbors as a crater that grows with energy**. The surplus that reaches surviving rim cells naturally becomes *their* fling (pushing the remaining body) and cracks — which is your idea, but it also makes a big blast carve a big crater instead of always one cell. And I'll **decouple vaporization from brittleness**: blast carves the vaporize budget from the *full* incoming energy, so it works at any brittleness.

Let me show the new structure:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-26 21:09 UTC

Now verify: conservation still exact, vaporization works at brittleness 1, and crater grows with energy at brittleness 0.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-26 21:10 UTC

Both fixed, conservation intact:

- **Issue 1 (no near-impact vaporize at brittleness 1):** vaporization is now carved from the *full* incoming energy by blast, independent of brittleness. At brit 1 / blast 1, near-impact cells vaporize — 5 / 18 / 52 cells as energy goes 800 / 5000 / 30000.
- **Issue 2 (one cell lost regardless of impact):** when a cell vaporizes, the surplus beyond its threshold now **spills outward to intact neighbors** — the crater grows with energy (3 → 5 → 18 → 45 cells at brit 0 as energy climbs), and surplus that reaches the surviving rim becomes that fragment's fling instead of dying on a dust cell. Fling total now tracks energy (545 → 25,436) instead of capping at one cell.
- **Conservation:** still exact (0.0000% across 108 trials spanning the new spill paths).

This is a strict generalization of your suggestion: the surplus does become fling on the remaining fragment — but routed through a crater that expands first, so a bigger blast carves a bigger hole and only the leftover at the rim pushes the surviving body. The net effect you wanted (energy no longer vanishes when a cell vaporizes) holds, and `blast` now reads as "crater size" while `brittleness` reads as "cracks vs local fling" for whatever energy doesn't vaporize.

One consequence worth feeling for: because vaporizing a cell only consumes its mass-threshold and spills the rest, the **vaporize channel stays small** (it's just the latent "cost" of the pulverized mass) while most energy shows up as **fling** — which is physically how a blast works (pulverizing is cheap; throwing the debris is where the energy goes). If you'd rather vaporization *consume* more of the energy (deeper but smaller craters), that's a one-line knob on how much the spill carries vs. how much the threshold eats — say the word and I'll wire it.

## 🧑 User · 2026-06-26 21:24 UTC

Wait, the vaporisation surplus should actually normally traverse the graph, i.e. go to the outward channel. I noticed that as it is now, if the hit is strong enough and there is lots of surplus energy, the leftover just spreads uniformly independently of  the toughness and directionality of the hit, what do you think, is this intended?

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-26 21:26 UTC

You're right, and it's not intended — good catch. Right now vaporize surplus goes through a separate `spill()` that divides energy **uniformly** among neighbors and bypasses the bond graph entirely. So a strong hit's leftover ignores both directionality (no forward focus) and toughness (no bonds to break), which is exactly the washout you noticed.

The fix is to stop treating vaporization as a special radial spill and instead route the surplus through the **normal transmit channel** — the same alignment-weighted, bond-breaking propagation a surviving cell uses. That unifies the model: a vaporized cell is just a cell that turns to dust and puts **all** its surplus into transmit (no local fling, since it has no fragment), while a surviving cell splits into fling + transmit. The crater still grows with energy, but now it grows *forward* (directionality) and is gated by *toughness* along the way.

Let me read the current propagate and restructure it.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-27 14:02 UTC

All builds green. Here's how to control asteroid impacts now:

## The fix

Asteroid↔asteroid collisions fed the **real asteroid masses** (thousands) into `E = ½·m_eff·v_n²·(1−e²)`, so even a slow touch produced enormous energy — total shatter. I added **`fracture.asteroidImpactScale`** (default `0.002`) that scales those masses down to the same effective-impactor range bullets use (`bulletMass = 0.001`). It's the main "how hard do bumps hit" knob.

## Your three levers (all in the new **Fracture** tab / `fracture` config)

1. **`asteroidImpactScale`** (0.002) — the big one. Lower it → collisions deposit less energy → bumps chip instead of shatter. This is what was missing. Try `0.0005–0.005`.
2. **`asteroidCollisionThreshold`** (20) — minimum approach speed before a collision fractures at all. Raise it so gentle contacts do *nothing*.
3. **`asteroidBlastFraction`** (0.08) — how much of a collision's energy craters vs cracks.

Tune them live in the demo's **Fracture** tab (changes apply immediately) and watch the `L` ForceLog's `E=…` line on a collision — aim for `E` comparable to a bullet hit's, i.e. in the low thousands rather than hundreds-of-thousands.

One caveat worth flagging: the collision currently scales *both* bodies' impactor mass by the same factor, and uses the closing speed `v_n`. A fast head-on between two big rocks will still shatter both hard (correct) — `asteroidImpactScale` sets the overall sensitivity, while `asteroidCollisionThreshold` carves off the gentle end. If you want big rocks to *resist* better than small ones specifically, that'd be a per-size tweak — say the word and I can make the scale depend on body mass.

I'm wondering why we need this impact coefficient for asteroids when we could just tune the mass of the bullet so they do comparatively similar damage. ## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-27 14:20 UTC

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-27 14:25 UTC

Verified the key number: asteroid mass = `Density · Σ(Area·DensityMult)`. With areas in px² (~1500/cell) a 30-cell rock weighs **~60,000**, while bond strengths are **~600**. That ~100× mismatch is the root of all three of your observations. Here's the honest breakdown.

## 1. You're right — `AsteroidImpactScale` is the same fudge we refactored out

The real problem isn't asteroids specifically; it's that **two incompatible scales coexist**: physical mass (`Area·Density` ≈ 10⁴–10⁵) and bond strength / fracture energy (≈ 10²–10³). Energy is `½·m_eff·v²`, so anything using real mass produces runaway `E`. The bullet *hides* this by using a fake tiny `bulletMass = 0.001` — which is itself a fudge. Asteroid collisions can't, because their mass is real physics (used for collision response + inertia), so I papered over it with `AsteroidImpactScale`. Two fudges for one underlying unit mismatch.

The clean fix is to reconcile the two scales **once**, globally, and delete both fudges. Two equivalent ways:

- **(A) One global `energyScale`**: `E = energyScale · ½·m_eff·v²` for *every* impact, using **real masses everywhere** (real bullet mass, real asteroid mass). `energyScale` is just a unit conversion from physical kinetic energy → fracture-energy units — not a per-interaction knob. Least churn.
- **(B) Raise material strengths ~100×** (`toughness`, `kVapor`, `surfaceEnergyPerLen`) to physical scale, set a realistic `bulletMass`, delete the scales. More re-tuning, same result.

I'd do **(A)**. One caveat to be explicit about: with real masses, a fast heavy-rock collision *is* physically far more energetic than a bullet — so collisions stay punchy, and the only collision-specific knob left is `asteroidCollisionThreshold` (the gentle-bump cutoff). If you instead want bumps *artificially* gentler than physics implies, that genuinely requires a dampener (the thing you dislike). My read is you want (A) + threshold. Want me to wire it — `energyScale` global, real masses, delete `AsteroidImpactScale` and the `bulletMass` fudge?

## 2. Piercing: thin deep channel vs. wide glancing crack

Not a bug — it's the directional model doing exactly what you set. Piercing has high `directionality` (0.85), so the transmit energy is funneled into the **forward** bonds: head-on it bores a *one-cell-wide channel straight in*, breaking a line of bonds that **doesn't disconnect anything** (a straight cut through a body doesn't detach a fragment unless it exits the far side) → looks like "little damage." Glancing, "forward" runs *along the surface*, so the energy fans across many surface bonds and **sheds chunks** → looks like more damage.

It's behaving like a real armor-piercing round (penetrates, doesn't shatter). To make head-on satisfying, two tuning options: lower piercing `directionality` so it fans enough to detach pieces, or raise its `blastFraction` so it **vaporizes a visible bored channel** (a hole) rather than an invisible hairline. I'd lean on `blastFraction` — that's the "it punched a hole through it" look. Want me to bump piercing's blast and ease its directionality as a starting point?

## 3. Per-cell accumulation wasn't deleted — it moved to bonds

It's still there, just relocated: the prototype model (which you approved) unified all damage accumulation onto **`Bond.Stress`** instead of `Cell.Damage`. Repeated fire on a spot still accumulates and eventually cracks (sustained fire), and `RelaxRate` still decays it. So the *feature* survives.

But you're right that there's a real **behavioral difference**, and it may be why it feels worse:
- **Per-cell** (old): a cell hit repeatedly *from any angle* accumulated toward its own ignition — forgiving, "chip the same spot and it crumbles."
- **Per-bond** (now): only the specific bonds that receive stress build up; hitting a spot from varied angles spreads stress across *different* bonds, so none may reach threshold — less forgiving.

If you liked the per-cell feel, I can **restore `Cell.Damage` as a second accumulator** layered on top of bonds: a cell sums incoming stress and, past a threshold, self-ignites (vaporizes or detaches) independent of which bonds took it. That gets back the "chip away at one spot" behavior without losing the bond model. Worth doing?

---

All three trace back to the scale mismatch (#1) — fixing that will also make #2 and #3 easier to tune because energies will be in a sane, consistent range. Shall I start with the (A) unification, then circle back to piercing and per-cell?

## 🧑 User · 2026-06-27 15:11 UTC

The piercing just isn't working as intended, it sometimes doesn't even trigger a fracture event when hitting something with its pointed head, while it can deal massive damage if it hits something sideways, with more surface colliding. What does surfaceEnergyPerLen do? 

Before choosing one of the 2 fixes, I would like to review some things: 
1. Per cell stress/damage accumulation should definitely be reintroduced, alongside the bond accumulation. 
2. In your comparison you take the whole asteroids mass and compare it to the strength of a single bond, considering there are ~100/300 bonds in a 30-cell rock they should have the same scale, am I wrong? If not, the bond strength should just be increased to be on the same scale as the density.
3. The BlastFraction should be taken from the local energy per-cell, not from the total budget. We introduced this to fix the edgecase of brittleness = 1. In the old model, the outward energy portion was a lerp(0.1,0.96,brittleness). That should work, what do you think? 
4. When something vaporises, shouldn't there be some energy loss (apart from the energy needed to vaporise the cell itself)? Right now, setting the BlastFraction to 1.0 makes the fracturing completely ignore the material brittleness, although I think this would be fixed by implementing the previous point i made.

What do you think of this? Do you agree, would you do it differently? How does real physics work in this case?

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-27 15:16 UTC

Good — these are the right things to scrutinize. Let me answer the concrete question first, then go through your four points with where I agree and where I'd push back, plus the physics.

## `surfaceEnergyPerLen`

When a bond breaks, the energy consumed is `min(share, surfaceEnergyPerLen · EdgeLength)`. It's the **Griffith surface energy** — the cost, per pixel of new crack, of the propagating wave passing through a bond. Higher → each break drains more of the wave → cracks **arrest sooner** (shorter reach). It's distinct from `Strength`: `Strength` is the *stress threshold* to break a bond; `surfaceEnergyPerLen` is the *energy the break costs the wave*. (Right now they're decoupled, which is why you can have a tough body that, once it does crack, still propagates far, or vice-versa.)

## The piercing bug

I think it's the **same focused-channel effect, taken to its extreme** — and your points 3/4 below will largely fix it. High `directionality` funnels almost the entire transmit into the *single* most-aligned forward bond, so a head-on hit bores a **one-cell-wide line of broken bonds that never disconnects a fragment** → `CountComponents` stays 1 → the process is dropped as a no-op → "no fracture event." Sideways, "forward" runs along the surface, the energy fans across many bonds, chunks detach → big visible damage. So it's not random; it's geometry. (If it still misfires after the rework, I'll check the collision *contact generation* too — a sharp head-on can produce a degenerate single-point manifold — but I'd bet on the channel effect.)

## Your four points

**1. Per-cell accumulation back, alongside bonds — agree.** Re-add `Cell.Damage`: a cell sums incoming stress and, past a per-material threshold, self-ignites (detaches/pulverizes) regardless of which bonds took the hits. That restores "chip the same spot from any angle and it crumbles," which bond-only accumulation loses.

**2. The scale comparison — you're right, my single-bond framing was misleading.** Total bond strength (~200 bonds × ~600 ≈ 120k) *is* the same order as the mass (~60k). The real mismatch is in **energy**: `E = ½·m·v²`, and the `v²` blows it up — even a slow collision's `E` (10⁶⁺) dwarfs the *total* fracture cost (~10⁴). So the fix isn't a per-interaction fudge; it's putting **energy and strength on one scale and using real masses everywhere**. Then the threshold *emerges from physics*: a gentle bump has `E < strength` → nothing breaks; fast → shatters. One nuance: "raise bond strength" alone isn't enough — `surfaceEnergyPerLen` (and the `E` itself) have to move to the same scale too. Cleanest as **one global `energyScale` divisor on `E`** (least churn, leaves authored material values alone) *or* raising `toughness`+`surfaceEnergyPerLen` together — mathematically the same. Either way: delete `AsteroidImpactScale` **and** the `bulletMass` fudge.

**3. Blast from local dump + `lerp(0.1, 0.96, brittleness)` — agree, it's better than what I did.** Small correction: blast is *already* taken per-cell-local in the current code (`vaporize = blast · e`, `e` = that cell's energy). The valuable part is the **lerp**. Replacing my "carve vaporize from the full `e` first" hack with:
```
transmit = lerp(0.1, 0.96, brittleness) · e   → bonds
dump     = (1 − transmit) · e                 → local: vaporize = blast·dump, fling = rest
```
…fixes *both* edge cases at once: the floor (0.04 local at brittleness 1) means a brittle body still vaporizes a little near the impact (the original issue 1), and because brittleness now always governs the transmit/dump split, `blast = 1` no longer bypasses it (your issue 4). Cleaner than the decoupling I bolted on. I'd expose the `0.1`/`0.96` floors as config so they're tunable.

**4. Energy loss on vaporization — agree, and it's the *physical* answer.** Pulverizing material is extremely dissipative: most of the energy goes to comminution (new surface area) + heat + flash, not to throwing the dust. So vaporization should be a real **sink**: consume the threshold (comminution) plus a fraction of the surplus to heat (`vaporEfficiency < 1`), and only the remainder becomes ejecta fling. That naturally tames `blast = 1` (it bleeds energy instead of propagating it) and, combined with #3, keeps brittleness always relevant.

The one thing I'd insist on: **keep the loss *named*, not vanishing.** My whole argument for the refactor was "you can always say where the energy went." A loss channel labeled "vaporize/heat" in the readout preserves that — it's legible dissipation, not the old silent disappearance. With that amendment I fully agree; strict conservation-into-motion was cleaner on paper but less physical than what you're proposing.

## How real physics actually splits it

An impact's kinetic energy partitions into: **elastic rebound** (your `restitution`), **Griffith surface energy** (creating crack area — the cleavage channel), **comminution** (pulverizing to fragments/dust — a *large* sink at high energy), **plastic deformation + heat + sound + light** (pure losses), and **kinetic energy of the ejecta/fragments** (fling). Brittle materials route more into cleavage cracks and less into plastic flow; ductile ones absorb more as plastic/heat and crack less. A crack advances while the energy-release-rate stays above the material's toughness and **arrests** when it drops below — exactly the budget-runs-out behavior. So real fracture is **lossy**; named heat/comminution sinks are correct, and your point 4 is more faithful than my conservative version.

## What I'd do

I agree with all four, with the two amendments (energyScale as the scale fix in #2; keep the vaporize loss *named* in #4). The combined per-cell rule:

```
transmit = lerp(reachMin, reachMax, brittleness) · e        → bonds (directional)
dump     = (1 − transmit) · e
  vaporTry = blast · dump
  if vaporTry ≥ thr:  vaporize_loss += thr + (1−vaporEff)·(vaporTry−thr)   // SINK (named)
                      flingE += vaporEff·(vaporTry − thr)                   // ejecta
  else:               flingE += vaporTry                                    // shove, not destroyed
  flingE += (1 − blast) · dump
```
plus real masses + global `energyScale` (#2) and per-cell `Damage` (#1).

Since this changes the **core propagation**, I'd strongly prefer to land it in the **HTML prototype (v4) first** so you can feel it — especially the brittleness×blast interaction and the new loss channel on the readout bar — before I touch the engine again. Want me to update v4 with the lerp split + named vaporize loss + per-cell damage, and add an "energyScale"/real-mass toggle so you can confirm the scale unification, then port once it feels right?

## 🧑 User · 2026-06-27 15:24 UTC

Yes, let's do it in the prototype first.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-27 15:25 UTC

## 🧑 User · 2026-06-27 15:39 UTC

How is the surplus from vaporisation (minus the lost energy) handled in the prototype? I see that it doesn't become outward energy as in the game, vaporisation efficiency just transfroms fling energy into lost energy.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-27 15:40 UTC

You're exactly right, and it's a regression I introduced with the lerp rework. Let me show the current vaporize branch:

```js
if (gate) {
  cells[i].vapor = true;
  const comminution = Math.min(dump, thr);
  ch.vaporize += comminution;
  const rest = dump - comminution;
  ch.heat += (1 - veff) * rest;          // lost
  const ejecta = veff * rest;
  cells[i].flingE += ejecta; ch.fling += ejecta;   // ← LOCAL fling, not outward
}
```

The surviving surplus (`veff·rest`) goes to the dust cell's **local fling**, so `vaporEff` only slides energy between fling and heat — it never re-enters the outward wave. In the engine version a vaporized cell sends its whole surplus (`e − thr`) **outward as transmit** (that's what grows the crater forward). My lerp rework split `transmit` out *first* and then trapped the dump locally, breaking that.

The fix: route the surviving surplus into **`transmit`** (the outward wave), with `(1−veff)` lost to heat. Then `vaporEff` becomes the blast wave's **penetration efficiency** — high = punches deep, low = absorbed locally as heat — which is the meaningful behavior, and it restores forward crater growth.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-27 15:41 UTC

Now verify conservation still holds and that `vaporEff` now drives outward reach (not just a fling↔heat trade):

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-27 15:41 UTC

Fixed and verified. `vaporEff` now controls the **blast wave's penetration**, not a cosmetic fling↔heat trade:

| vaporEff | vaporized cells | broken bonds | heat (lost) |
|---|---|---|---|
| 0 | 5 | 9 | 16,521 |
| 0.3 | 12 | 19 | 14,184 |
| 0.6 | 17 | 25 | 10,055 |
| 1.0 | 25 | 47 | 0 |

At `vaporEff = 0` the surplus is fully absorbed as heat → tiny shallow crater; at `1.0` it all continues outward → deep crater + far cracks, no loss. Conservation still exact (`break + vaporize + fling + heat = E`, 0.0000% across 81 cases).

So the model now: each vaporizing cell consumes its comminution threshold (`vaporize` channel), bleeds `(1−vaporEff)` of the surplus to `heat`, and sends the rest **outward through the transmit channel** — which is what grows the crater forward and matches the engine's "surplus travels on," now with the physical heat sink layered in. The dust particles' visual motion is driven separately (random outward), so vaporized cells contributing 0 local fling doesn't hurt the look.

Give it a feel — `vaporEff` should read as "how deep does the blast punch" now. When it's right, I'll fold all of this (lerp split, per-cell fatigue, named heat sink, outward surplus) into the engine port along with the #2 scale unification.

You found a real bug. Look at the fatigue accumulator in the current code:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-27 16:13 UTC

Matches your spec now, and the bug is gone:

- **`blast = 0` vaporizes nothing** even at E=30000 (was vaporizing via the full-dump fatigue path).

The flow is exactly your tree:

```
e
├─ outward = lerp(reachMin, reachMax, brittleness)·e        → bonds, biased by (weapon+material dir)/2
└─ dump    = rest
   ├─ vaporiseEnergy = blast·dump
   │    ├─ comminution → accumulates toward cellToughness·mass   [vaporize, loss]
   │    └─ once threshold reached, surplus splits:
   │         ├─ (1−vaporEff)·surplus                              [heat, loss]
   │         └─ vaporEff·surplus → added to outward               (penetration)
   └─ fling = (1−blast)·dump                                      [local kinetic]
```

The one deliberate refinement vs your written `(1−brittleness)%`: the local/outward split uses `lerp(reachMin, reachMax, brittleness)` rather than raw `1−brittleness`, so the floors keep brittleness 0 and 1 from being degenerate (that was the fix for blast bypassing brittleness). And `cellToughness` is now the single vaporize/comminution threshold (the old separate `KVAPOR` instant-gate is gone), defaulting to a moderate value so a direct full-blast hit pulverizes while weak hits chip over time.

## What landed in the engine

**Propagation model** (matching the approved prototype, in [FractureKernel.StepFront](GameEngine/Engine/Destruction/FractureKernel.cs)):
- **Lerp split** — `transmit = lerp(reachMin, reachMax, brittleness)·e`, so brittleness always governs outward-vs-local and `blast = 1` can't bypass it.
- **Portioned blast + comminution fatigue** — `vaporize = blast·dump` (so `blast = 0` vaporizes nothing); comminution accumulates per-cell in the re-added **`Cell.Damage`** toward `cellToughness·mass`; the cell pulverizes when reached (instant *and* sustained-fire paths unified).
- **Named heat loss + outward surplus** — a pulverized cell's surplus splits `(1−vaporEff)` to heat (lost) and `vaporEff` back into `transmit` (penetration → crater grows forward).
- **Per-cell + per-bond relax** — `StressRelaxSystem` now decays both `Cell.Damage` and `Bond.Stress`.

**Scale unification (#2)** — [FractureService.ComputeEnergy](GameEngine/Engine/Destruction/FractureService.cs) now applies one global **`energyScale`** to `½·m_eff·v²` with **real masses everywhere**. Deleted both fudges (`AsteroidImpactScale`, `PiercingImpactScale`); `bulletMass` is now a real tuned mass (10), asteroid collisions and piercing use real masses, and gentle bumps fall below bond strength naturally.

**Config** — `MaterialConfig.CellToughness` added; `FractureGlobalConfig` gained `energyScale`/`reachMin`/`reachMax`/`vaporEff` and dropped `kVapor`/`asteroidImpactScale`/`piercingImpactScale`; pushed via `ApplyFractureTuning`. Demo **Fracture** tab + **Material** sliders updated; [game_config.json](GameEngine/Assets/game_config.json) migrated (per-material `cellToughness`, reworked `fracture` block).

## The calibration you'll want to do
This is the part I can't tune without playing. The new **`energyScale`** (0.0001) and **`bulletMass`** (10) together set absolute damage, and they interact with the now-real collision masses. Open the demo's **Fracture** tab, watch the `L` ForceLog `E=…` line, and:
1. Set `energyScale` so a cannon shot's `E` lands near a rock's bond strengths (rock cracks, titanium needs sustained fire).
2. Check asteroid collisions feel right at the *same* `energyScale` (slow bumps below threshold, fast ones shatter) — no separate knob now, which is the point.
3. Tune `cellToughness` per material for crater/vaporize feel, and `vaporEff` for crater depth.

## 🧑 User · 2026-06-27 19:11 UTC

I'm thinking of taking a step back and revisit the fracturing model. 
Shouldn't the bonds perpendicular to the energy traversal break? Not the ones that simply let the energy pass (although they should still be damaged), but the ones that have to keep the cells moved by the passing energy still and resist that movement. This would mean that energy moves according to the directionality, but the damaged bonds should be the ones perpendicular to the direction the energy flows, even though energy doesn't pass in that direction. I need your honest opinion on this, if it's realistic and if it would make sense to implement.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-27 19:13 UTC

Honest answer: **yes — and you've actually identified that the current model is the *less* physical one.** This is a real and well-understood distinction in fracture mechanics, and I think it's the single biggest realism win on the table.

## The physics

Picture cells on a grid, a crack running left→right (radially out from an impact). The crack is a *line of separation*: cells above it part from cells below it. The bonds it severs are the **vertical** ones spanning that line — i.e. the bonds **perpendicular** to the propagation direction. The crack tip advances in +x by breaking the next perpendicular bond, not by breaking the +x bonds. Breaking the +x (parallel) bonds — what we do now — is geometrically "eat a tunnel of cells," which is exactly the boring/piercing artifact you've been fighting.

And the driving force is right there too: a point impact puts the surrounding material into **hoop (circumferential) tension**, and brittle stuff fails in tension. The tensile stress is tangential, so it tears the tangential bonds → **radial cracks**. That's the star-fracture you get hitting glass with a hammer. Your description — "the perpendicular bonds resist the lateral movement and break, while the parallel ones just pass the energy (damaged but mostly intact)" — is textbook Mode-I radial cracking. The parallel bonds mostly see *compression* (which bonds resist well) and conduct the wave; the perpendicular ones see the tension and snap.

So: **energy travels along the directionality; the breaks happen perpendicular to it.** Decoupling "where energy goes" from "what breaks" is both more correct and conceptually cleaner than what we have.

## Why it's worth doing

- **Fixes piercing organically.** A focused shot drives energy straight in → severs the transverse bonds along that line → a clean planar split, instead of an invisible tunnel that detaches nothing. A blunt shot radiates → radial star. Directionality finally reads as "clean cut ↔ shatter," which is intuitive.
- **Fragments actually separate.** Two radial cracks reaching a free surface isolate the wedge between them — real detachment, where parallel-breaking left everything connected.
- **Synergy with spin pre-stress**, which already weakens *tangential* bonds — exactly the ones radial cracking breaks. A fast spinner radially cleaving easier falls out for free.

## Honest concerns / how I'd build it

- **It's a core rewrite of `StepFront`'s break step**, so prototype in v4 first. The shape: for cell `i` with a local flow direction (from the cell that delivered the energy → `i`), split its bonds by `align = dot(dir_to_neighbor, flow)`: **transmit** through the high-`|align|` (parallel) bonds as now, but accumulate **breaking stress** on the low-`|align|` (perpendicular) ones, drawn from the local energy as the surface-energy channel. Still conservative — we just reassign which bonds the break-energy spends on.
- **Flow direction must be local, not global.** Use "direction energy arrived from," so radial flow produces radial cracks (a single global impact dir only works for a straight crack).
- **Tune so it doesn't sever *every* side bond.** The perpendicular breaking should fade as the wave attenuates, or you'd shear the whole neighborhood. That falls out if the breaking stress scales with the (decaying) local energy.
- **One thing to watch:** purely-perpendicular breaking under-produces circumferential/hoop cracks (spalling), which come from reflected tension — more complex. Predominantly-radial is the right primary behavior, but I'd keep a small parallel-break term as a knob so you can dial in some of both.

My recommendation: do it, but **prototype it in v4 first** (it changes the feel a lot), with a slider blending perpendicular-vs-parallel breaking so we can compare against the current model side by side before committing to another engine port. Want me to spin that up?

## 🧑 User · 2026-06-27 19:22 UTC

Let's do it, but I want to understand it better first: the only thing that should change is: 
1. recompute the direction of energy flow at every step, not the direction of the primary hit.
2. outward energy flow partitioning stays the same, only the damaged bonds change, and I want to understand how it would work with our energy-conserving model, how would you decide how much energy simply flows and how much goes into damaging the resisting bonds? How would you distribute the energy that goes into damaging among resisting bonds?

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-27 19:25 UTC

Good — let me lay out the mechanics precisely so we're aligned before I touch v4. Your two points are exactly the change; here's how each piece works in the conserving model.

## 1. Local flow direction (the easy part)

Right now `ImpactDirLocal` is the global hit direction. Instead, each cell records **who delivered its energy** (its parent in the flood). The flow direction at cell `i` is then `normalize(i.centroid − parent.centroid)` — recomputed per cell. The struck cell uses the impact direction (no parent). So `deliver(j, …)` also stores `parent[j] = i`. That's it for point 1: radial impacts get radial flow, a focused shot gets a straight flow line.

## 2. The energy budget at a cell

A cell receives energy `e`. The split into **local vs outward** is unchanged:

```
dump   = (1 − transmitFrac)·e        // fling + vaporize, exactly as now
budget =      transmitFrac·e         // the outward energy
transmitFrac = lerp(reachMin, reachMax, brittleness)
```

What changes is what `budget` *does*. For every intact bond `i→k`, with `align = dot(dir_ik, flow)`:
- **conductor weight** `wc_k = max(0, align)^exp` — carries the wave forward (this is today's directional partitioning, **unchanged**)
- **resistor weight** `wr_k = 1 − |align|` — peaks at 90°; these are the bonds that crack

## How much flows vs. how much damages — make it *emergent*, not a fixed split

This is the key answer, and I'd argue strongly for the **Griffith** approach rather than a "X% flows, Y% breaks" knob:

The budget is **applied as stress** to the resistor bonds (free — stress is just a threshold check, elastically stored). A resistor bond only **costs energy when it actually breaks**, and then it costs its surface energy `eSurf = surfaceEnergyPerLen · edgeLength`. So:

```
spent = 0
for each resistor bond k (most-stressed first):
    bond.stress += budget · (wr_k / Σwr) · spinMul_k     // shear from the passing wave (elastic)
    if bond.stress ≥ bond.strength and spent < budget:
        break bond k
        eSurf = min(surfaceEnergyPerLen·len_k, budget − spent)
        spent += eSurf                                    // the only energy the break costs

budget −= spent          // energy consumed creating crack surface
// remaining budget flows forward, partitioned by wc_k → deliver(forward neighbours)
```

So **"how much flows vs damages" is decided by how much crack surface actually forms** — a finite hit can only create finite surface (`spent` is capped at `budget`), and whatever's left propagates. A strong hit breaks many resistor bonds (consumes more) yet still has plenty left to travel; a weak hit breaks few and mostly flows. This is exactly Griffith's energy-release criterion, and it's why I'd avoid an arbitrary flow/break fraction — the physics gives you the split for free, and it stays conserving: `e = dump + spent(break) + forwardFlow`.

Crucially, a resistor bond that breaks **does not conduct** — the crack face doesn't pass energy across it. Energy only ever moves forward through conductors; the budget reaching a forward neighbour is `budget − spent`.

## How the damage distributes among resistor bonds

The stress each resistor bond receives is **perpendicularity-weighted and normalized**: `budget · wr_k / Σwr`. The most-perpendicular bonds (true 90° to the flow) get the most, so they crack first — which is what carves the crack *along* the flow line. Sub-threshold stress just accumulates (the fatigue you already have), so repeated waves through the same spot eventually open it.

And your "conductors should still be damaged" point: I'd add a small conductor stress term, `bond.stress += kConductor · share_forward`, with `kConductor` small (≈0.1). So forward bonds fray slowly under sustained flow but the *primary* break is perpendicular.

## One worked intuition

- **Focused shot, straight in:** flow is a straight line; at each cell the two side (≈90°) bonds take almost all the resistor stress and snap, while the forward bond conducts. Result: a clean planar crack slicing along the shot — the body splits in two. (Today this just tunnels.)
- **Blunt shot:** energy radiates; flow is radial everywhere; tangential bonds crack along each radial → a star fracture, wedges detaching where radials reach the rim.

The only genuinely new parameter is `kConductor` (conductor fray). `wr_k = 1−|align|` and the Griffith consumption reuse what's already there. The normalization choice (`/Σwr`) is the one tunable I'd want to feel — it keeps reach mesh-stable but you may want it stronger.

Does this match your mental model? If so I'll build it into v4 with a **perpendicular↔parallel break blend** slider (so you can A/B against the current parallel-breaking) plus the `kConductor` knob, and we feel it before porting.

## 🧑 User · 2026-06-27 19:34 UTC

So sub-threshold bonds that don't break accumulate stress, but don't subtract anything from the budget of the fracture? Isn't this in contradiction with the whole energy-conserving model? Also, couldn't the conductors be damaged just like all the other bonds (using the same formula), so the bonds that aren't perfectly aligned with the hit receive some damage but still work as conductors.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-27 19:37 UTC

Both points are correct, and the second one actually fixes the first. Let me concede and reframe.

## You're right — "free" sub-threshold stress breaks conservation

My "stress is free until it breaks" was a cheat. Permanent damage is irreversible, so it *must* cost energy — only *reversible elastic* strain returns to the wave, and if I let stress accumulate (which is the whole point, for fatigue) that's irreversible. So: **stressing a bond consumes energy, subtracted from the budget.** And once stressing costs energy, the cleanest move is to unify the two quantities I'd kept separate:

> **A bond's `strength` becomes its energy-to-break.** Stress accumulates the absorbed energy; the bond breaks when accumulated ≥ strength. No separate `surfaceEnergyPerLen` / `eSurf` term — the energy spent damaging it *is* the surface energy.

That's one fewer parameter and it's fully conserving: every joule a bond absorbs is accounted, whether it eventually breaks or just sits there damaged.

## And yes — one formula for every bond

This is the better structure. Don't split bonds into "conductors" and "resistors" — give every bond **both** roles by orientation, from the same budget:

```
budget = transmitFrac · e            // outward energy (dump already removed)
flow   = normalize(i − parent)        // recomputed per step

W = 0
for each intact bond k → neighbour:
    align = dot(dir_ik, flow)
    wc_k  = max(0, align)^exp          // conduct weight  (alignment)
    wd_k  = 1 − |align|                // damage weight   (perpendicularity)
    W += wc_k + wd_k

for each intact bond k:
    absorb  = budget · wd_k / W        // CONSUMED  → damage
    forward = budget · wc_k / W        // TRANSMITTED → neighbour
    bond.stress += absorb · spinMul_k  // strength = energy-to-break
    if bond.stress ≥ bond.strength:  break bond        // its `forward` is consumed by the break
    else:                            deliver(neighbour_k, forward)
```

Because `wc` and `wd` are normalized over the **same** `W`, the budget splits exactly: `Σabsorb (damage) + Σforward (flow) = budget`. Conserved, no free lunch.

Now your two questions answer themselves:
- **How much flows vs. damages** is no longer a knob or a Griffith cap — it's set by the **bond geometry around the cell**. A cell whose bonds are mostly perpendicular to the flow dumps most of its budget into damage; one with a clean forward bond passes most of it on. Radial flow + side bonds → sides absorb and crack while the forward bond conducts. Exactly the radial-crack behavior, emergent.
- **A bond at, say, 45°** gets `wd = 0.29` and `wc ≈ 0.6` — so it **absorbs some damage *and* conducts**, which is your point: not-perfectly-aligned bonds fray but still pass energy. Perfectly aligned → pure conductor; perfectly perpendicular → pure absorber.

## One consequence to flag
`exp` (the `AlignExponent`) now controls the conduct/damage balance directly: high `exp` makes only near-aligned bonds conduct, so more of the budget is forced into damage → shorter, more shattery cracks; low `exp` lets off-axis bonds conduct → longer, cleaner cracks. So that one knob meaningfully shapes reach-vs-shatter now, and `surfaceEnergyPerLen` is gone (merged into `strength`).

If this matches your model, I'll build it into v4: the unified per-bond split, `strength` as energy-to-break, per-step recomputed flow, plus a blend slider to A/B against the current parallel-breaking. Sound right?

## 🧑 User · 2026-06-27 19:40 UTC

where does directionality come into play?

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-27 19:41 UTC

Good catch — I dropped it in that last sketch. It belongs on the **conduct weight**, exactly where it was, and its job is now even clearer: **directionality shapes where the energy goes, and the perpendicular-damage rule just follows the resulting flow field.**

```
align = dot(dir_ik, flow)
wc_k  = lerp(1, max(0, align)^exp, directionality)   // ← directionality here
wd_k  = 1 − |align|                                   // damage stays perpendicularity-only
```

- **directionality = 0** → `wc_k = 1` for every neighbour → energy fans out **omnidirectionally**. Each downstream cell's flow points radially outward (from its parent), so its perpendicular = tangential bonds crack → a **radial star**.
- **directionality = 1** → `wc_k = max(0,align)^exp` → a **tight forward cone**, energy stays on the impact line, flow is straight, perpendicular bonds along it snap → a **clean planar split**.

So directionality controls the crack *pattern* (star ↔ line) purely by steering the conduction; the "break perpendicular to local flow" mechanism is invariant. That's a nice separation of concerns — and it's why a sharp piercing round (high directionality) will finally cut a clean line instead of tunnelling, while a blunt grenade (low directionality) shatters radially.

`wd_k` deliberately does **not** see directionality — perpendicular is perpendicular regardless of how focused the hit is. And the effective value stays `(weapon.Directionality + material.CrackDirectionality) / 2`, as today.

One interaction worth noting: at low directionality a perpendicular bond has both `wc≈1` (conducts) and `wd=1` (absorbs), so it splits its share ~half to lateral spread and half to cracking — which is what *feeds* the radial fan (lateral conduction seeds neighbours whose own flow is radial). At high directionality those same side bonds get `wc≈0`, so they only crack. So the same two weights give you the whole spectrum.

That's the complete picture now: per-step flow, unified per-bond `absorb`(=damage, consumed)/`forward`(=flow) split normalized over one `W`, `strength` = energy-to-break, and directionality on the conduct weight. Want me to build this into v4 with the A/B blend slider against the current parallel-breaking?

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-27 19:54 UTC

It's in the prototype — [fracture-cells-v4.html](prototypes/fracture-cells-v4.html). Here's what changed and how to feel it.

## What I built

- **Per-step flow direction** — each cell now derives its flow from the cell that delivered its energy (`parent`), not the global impact direction. Radial spread gives radial flow; a focused beam gives straight flow.
- **One unified per-bond split** — every intact bond, by its alignment to the local flow, splits its share of the outward budget into:
  - `absorb = budget·wd/W` → **consumed as damage** (`bond.stress += absorb`), and
  - `forward = budget·wc/W` → **transmitted onward**,
  
  normalized over the same `W`, so it's conserving (no free stress — your point). `wc = lerp(1, align^exp, directionality)` (conduct), `wd = lerp(|align|, 1−|align|, breakPerp)` (damage).
- **`strength` = energy-to-break** — `surfaceEnergyPerLen`/`eSurf` are gone; the energy a bond absorbs *is* its surface energy.
- **Directionality** sits on the conduct weight, shaping the flow field (star ↔ line); damage is always perpendicular to that flow.
- **New sliders**: **Break perp.** (the A/B: 0 = old break-along-flow tunneling, 1 = new break-perpendicular), and **Align exp.** (conduct cone sharpness / reach↔shatter). Removed the now-redundant blocked-crack toggle.

## Verified headless
- Conservation `break+vaporize+fling+heat = E` exact (0.0000%, 162 cases).
- Directionality: dir 0 → spread (star), dir 1 → elongated along flow (line).
- breakPerp shifts breaks from parallel→perpendicular (the metric understates it — irregular Voronoi — so trust the visual).

## To feel it
1. **Break perp. = 0 vs 1** on the same shot — the headline A/B. At 0 you should see the old tunnel; at 1 the crack should cleave *across* the flow.
2. **Perp + Directionality sweep** — high dir should cut a clean line (finally fixes piercing-style hits); low dir should radial-star.
3. **Align exp.** — low = long clean cracks, high = shattery (more budget forced into damage).

## 🧑 User · 2026-06-27 20:05 UTC

Seems like there is lots less damage done, at an impact of 30000 energy it barely damages the surface of the asteroid (at minimum toughness and density). There must be something wrong because the damage should be exactly the same as before, just distributed differently. ## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-27 20:09 UTC

Two separate things here. The first is a real bug I introduced; the second is a fair criticism.

## The "much less damage" bug

It's not conservation working as intended — I **over-consume** energy on every break. Look at the current loop:

```js
const absorb = transmit * o.wd / W;
ch.break += absorb;                 // ← consumes the WHOLE directed amount
o.b.stress += absorb * spinMul;
if (o.b.stress >= o.b.strength) o.b.broken = true;
```

At min toughness a bond's `strength` might be ~40, but `absorb` is ~3000. The bond breaks the instant stress crosses 40 — yet I bill the **entire 3000** to the break channel and let none of it continue. So the first ring of bonds eats almost all the energy, nothing propagates, and you get a scratch. (Conservation *tests* still passed because the wasted energy was counted in `break` — it summed to 100%, it just went nowhere useful.)

The fix is the thing the old model actually did right: **a break only costs what it needs** (its surface energy = `strength`), and the **excess is recovered and keeps going**:

```js
const absorb  = transmit * o.wd / W;
const effStr  = o.b.strength / (1 + spinFactor(o.b));   // spin lowers the threshold
const needed  = max(0, effStr - o.b.stress);
const consumed = min(absorb, needed);                   // only pay to break
o.b.stress += consumed;  ch.break += consumed;
if (o.b.stress >= effStr) o.b.broken = true;
recovered += absorb - consumed;                          // over-damage continues
// forward = conduct budget + recovered, distributed by wc
```

This is still fully conserving (`Σconsumed + forward = budget`), but now breaking is *cheap* (you spend `strength` per bond, not the whole local budget), so a 30k hit cracks far and wide again — same damage level as before, just on the perpendicular bonds. It also makes the `break` channel finally read as real surface energy (small) with most of `E` going to fling, which is physically right.

Let me apply it:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-27 20:10 UTC

Now verify the damage is restored (lots of breaks at min toughness / high energy) and conservation holds:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-27 20:17 UTC

Damage is back and scales with energy again: at min toughness, broken bonds go **9 → 24 → 52** for E = 2k → 8k → 30k (out of 334), and the channels now read physically — `break` is small (1880 at 30k, real surface energy) while most of `E` goes to `fling` (22401). Conservation still exact (0.0000% across 144 cases). So the regression was the over-consumption bug, now fixed.

## On `Align exp.` — you're right, it largely overlaps directionality

They both control forward focus, just at different "stages":

- **Directionality (0→1)** blends the conduct weight between **omnidirectional** (`wc = 1` for every bond — energy sprays all ways → radial star) and **forward-focused** (`wc = max(0,align)^exp`). It's the "spread vs focus" dial. This is the real per-weapon/material knob.
- **Align exp.** only does anything *at* the focused end — it's the **exponent** that sets how *sharp* that forward cone is (`exp 1` = broad cosine lobe, `exp 4` = a narrow spike). At directionality 0 it does nothing.

So directionality answers "*how much* does energy focus," and alignExp answers "*how tight* is the focused cone" — related but not identical. The honest take: for tuning, directionality carries 90% of it, and alignExp is a secondary shape constant that mostly muddies the picture. I'd **drop the alignExp slider and bake it as a constant** (1.6), leaving directionality as the single focus knob — unless you specifically want cone-width control separate from focus-amount. 

Want me to remove the alignExp slider (keep it constant), or keep it exposed? Either way, give the fixed damage a feel — sweep energy and toughness and confirm a 30k hit now cracks deep, and try Break perp. 0↔1 again now that there's real propagation to see the pattern difference.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-27 20:35 UTC

Quick correction on your understanding first, because it's actually a bit off — and the difference matters for tuning.

The formula is:

```
wc = lerp(1, max(0, align)^alignExp, directionality)
```

So `alignExp` is the exponent of **`align`** (the bond's alignment with the flow, the dot product ∈ [−1,1]), **not** of directionality. It is *not* `directionality^alignExp`. Concretely:
- **`align`** = how aligned a bond is with the local flow. `alignExp` sharpens the forward cone: a forward bond (align≈1) always gets `^exp = 1`, but as a bond turns off-axis, a higher `alignExp` makes its weight fall off *faster* → a narrower beam.
- **`directionality`** is the **blend** between omnidirectional (`wc = 1` for every bond) and that cone (`align^alignExp`). At directionality 0, `wc = 1` everywhere regardless of `alignExp`; at 1, `wc = align^alignExp`.

So directionality = "how much it focuses," alignExp = "how tight the focused cone is." And it's per-bond `align`, not directionality, being raised to the power. (Also: it's not "smaller wc = more focused" — focus comes from the *contrast* in `wc` across bonds, i.e. aligned bonds keeping high wc while off-axis ones drop.)

Now the port. Let me read the current engine kernel and rewrite `StepFront` to match the prototype exactly.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-27 20:39 UTC

The perpendicular-breaking model is ported to the engine, exactly as the prototype. All four projects build clean, no dangling refs, and the game loads + runs.

## What changed in the engine

- **[FractureKernel.StepFront](GameEngine/Engine/Destruction/FractureKernel.cs)** — the bond section is now the prototype's unified model:
  - **Per-step flow**: `CrackFront.Parent[]` records who delivered each cell's energy; flow = `normalize(cell − parent)` (impact dir at the struck cell).
  - **One per-bond split**: `wc = lerp(1, max(0,align)^alignExp, directionality)` (conduct) and `wd = lerp(|align|, 1−|align|, breakPerp)` (damage), normalized over the same `W`.
  - **Consumed/recovered**: a bond only spends its `Strength` (= energy-to-break) to break; the over-damage is **recovered** and continues forward — this is the fix that restored full damage.
  - **`Strength` = energy-to-break**, `spinMul` lowers it (`effStr = Strength/spinMul`); `surfaceEnergyPerLen` is gone.
- **[PartitionFront](GameEngine/Engine/Destruction/FractureSimulator.cs)** remaps `Parent` across mid-fracture splits so continuing cracks keep their flow.
- **Config**: `FractureGlobalConfig.BreakPerp` added (drops `SurfaceEnergyPerLen`), pushed via `ApplyFractureTuning`; demo **Fracture** tab has a **Break Perp.** slider; [game_config.json](GameEngine/Assets/game_config.json) `fracture.breakPerp = 1.0`.

## And to close the loop on your question

`wc = lerp(1, max(0, align)^alignExp, directionality)` — `alignExp` is the exponent on **`align`** (per-bond alignment with flow), not on directionality, and it's not `directionality^alignExp`. Directionality is the *blend* (omni ↔ cone); alignExp is the cone's *sharpness*.

It's all live-tunable in the demo's **Fracture** tab now (`Break Perp.`, `Align Exp.`, `Directionality`, the energy/vapor/fling knobs). The same absolute-scale calibration note from the earlier port still applies — `energyScale` + `bulletMass` set overall damage against the real masses — so give it a pass in the demo and tune to taste. Anything else you want adjusted?

## 🧑 User · 2026-06-29 08:21 UTC

<ide_opened_file>The user opened the file /home/sebastian/ADVANCED/workspace/info/meta_notes.md in the IDE. This may or may not be related to the current task.</ide_opened_file>
I did some tuning on the material and weapons parameters, however the tuning tasks shouldn't be considered done yet. Here are some other things to adjust/add to the plan.
There is a problem with the computed energy from collision among fracturable bodies and it's almost certainly caused by the relativeVelocity computation. I noticed that the piercing round, even while traveling at a very high speed (2500 in the game_config) and having a respectable mass, sometimes generates very little energy (~100 or less) and bounces off of the impacted asteroid.

Does the system run before the CollisionSystem changes velocities? Does the relative velocity take into account the angular velocities of the bodies and the lever length? 

Regardless of this bug, this is what I would do:

Refactor of Fracture OnCollision, BeginFracture and ComputeEnergy
The clean fix is to change all three together:

OnCollision computes the true contact relative velocity and normal speed.
BeginFracture accepts the normal impact speed (or directly the impact energy) instead of an impactor velocity.
ComputeEnergy uses that speed without reconstructing velocities.

That produces a much simpler and less error-prone design, but it requires coordinated changes across all three functions.

The fact that OnCollision method of the FractureGameplay handles 3 unrelated behaviours (grenades, asteroid-on-asteroid, piercing round) isn't really SOLID. I'd split it like this:

GrenadeSystem — detonate grenades on contact.
ProjectileSystem — handle piercing-round impacts, projectile-specific fracture, ricochet, penetration, velocity clamping, etc.
FractureCollisionSystem (your current OnCollision) — handle collisions between ordinary fracturable rigid bodies and compute impact energy.

That separation follows the single-responsibility principle and makes each system much easier to reason about and extend.

There are also some visual bugs:
1. On fracturing, sometimes cracks appear and disappear some frames later, this shouldn't be possible since cracks are rendered only when a bond is broken, and that's irreversible.
2. The player shape HUD in the game, the cells should gradually become orange then red based on the damage absorbed by the cells, but this never happens.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-29 08:27 UTC

Both questions answered conclusively by the code. Let me give you the verdict, then fold everything into the plan.

## Your two diagnostic questions

**Q1 — Does fracture energy get computed before CollisionSystem changes velocities? No — that's the bug.** In [PlayingState.cs:92-96](GameEngine/Game/States/PlayingState.cs#L92), `CollisionSystem` runs, then `EventFlushSystem`. Inside `CollisionSystem.Update` it **publishes** the `CollisionEvent` during the narrow phase ([CollisionSystem.cs:121](GameEngine/Engine/Systems/CollisionSystem.cs#L121)) but **applies the velocity impulses afterward** in Phase 4 ([:125-132](GameEngine/Engine/Systems/CollisionSystem.cs#L125)). The bus is deferred, so `OnCollision` doesn't run until `EventFlushSystem` — *after* the solve. So it reads **post-bounce** velocities: the piercing round's approach speed has already been killed/reversed by the impulse, so `vRel` is tiny → `E ≈ 100` → it ricochets. Exactly your symptom.

**Q2 — Does relative velocity include angular + lever? Yes, the formula is fine.** `OnCollision` builds `vcA = vA.Linear + ωA×rA` and `vcB` likewise, and `ComputeEnergy`'s effective mass includes `(r×n)²/I`. So spin and lever arm are accounted for. The bug is purely the post-solve timing, not the formula — which is why your refactor (capture the *pre-solve* normal speed and pass it straight through) is the right fix.

The clean fix: `CollisionSystem` already knows the pre-solve normal approach speed (the `vn` it uses for the impulse bias in `GatherContact`) — carry it on the `CollisionEvent`, have `BeginFracture` take a **normal impact speed** (or energy) directly, and `ComputeEnergy` stop reconstructing velocities. Bullets pass their own normal speed (pre-anything). I agree with the SOLID split too.

I also traced both visual bugs:
- **Cracks vanish**: [WorldRenderer.cs:63-92](GameEngine/Gameplay/WorldRenderer.cs#L63) draws cracks from the live `FractureProcess.Broken[]`; when a hit breaks bonds but doesn't separate the body, `FractureCrackSystem` drops the process as a **no-op** and that `Broken[]` is discarded — so the cracks disappear. They should be rendered from the **persistent** `Bond.Stress ≥ Strength`.
- **HUD never reddens**: `DrawShipWidget` ([PlayingState.cs:699](GameEngine/Game/States/PlayingState.cs#L699)) fills alive cells a constant green and only ghost-outlines pulverized ones — there's no damage gradient at all. Needs per-cell damage (max `Bond.Stress/Strength` of its bonds) lerped green→orange→red.

Now let me add all of this to the plan.

## 🧑 User · 2026-06-29 08:52 UTC

For the piercing round, could we make it bounce much less than normal entities (to make it "pierce through" and not bounce) or does restitution alone handle this? If not we should add a new parameter to the weapon profile.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-29 08:52 UTC

Good question — let me check exactly how the bounce impulse uses restitution, because I suspect restitution alone won't give you "pierce through."

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-29 08:53 UTC

Here's the precise answer, and it changes the recommendation slightly.

**Restitution alone does *not* give you "pierce through."** Two things to note from the code:

- The pair restitution is `e = min(rbA.Restitution, rbB.Restitution)` ([CollisionSystem.cs:283](GameEngine/Engine/Systems/CollisionSystem.cs#L283)). So if the round's `RigidBody.Restitution = 0`, the combined `e` is 0 **regardless of the asteroid** — the bounce-*back* is already fully killed by restitution. You don't need a new param for "less bounce."
- **But** even at `e = 0`, `SolveContact` still applies the normal impulse `dPn = NormalMass·(0 − vn)` — it zeroes the round's *approach* velocity. So the round doesn't rebound, but it **stops dead at the surface**; it won't pass through. Restitution controls rebound, not penetration.

So to make it *pierce*, you have to suppress the collision **impulse itself**, not the restitution. That's a new mechanism — and it belongs on the **physics/collider side**, not the `WeaponProfile` (which only feeds the fracture model). Let me check whether the engine already has a sensor/trigger concept to build on.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-29 08:54 UTC

Confirmed: there's **no** sensor/trigger concept in the engine — every collider with a `RigidBody` gets the normal impulse. So:

## Answer

- **Restitution won't do it.** `min`-rule restitution `0` on the round already removes all bounce-back (no new param needed for that), but the round still gets **stopped at the surface** by the normal impulse. Restitution ≠ penetration.
- **You need a new parameter, but on the collider/physics side, not the WeaponProfile.** The clean form is a **`Collider.Sensor` flag**: a sensor still produces `CollisionEvent`s (so fracture fires) but `CollisionSystem` skips overlap-separation **and** the impulse for any pair involving it. The round then truly passes through.

With that, the new **`ProjectileSystem`** (from Task 0's split) owns the pierce feel:
- on each pierce → fracture the target + take the reciprocal self-fracture + bleed a *small* speed amount (a per-weapon `PenetrationDrag`/`SpeedLossPerPierce` knob for tuning) + stay on-axis (lateral clamp);
- it **stops when it self-shatters** — which happens quickly against a heavy/tough asteroid (big reciprocal energy), giving you exactly "only its own fracturing or a too-heavy asteroid stops it."

Bonus: making the round a sensor also sidesteps the energy bug *for the round specifically* — with no impulse, its velocity isn't mangled before fracture is computed.

So the parameter set: **`Collider.Sensor`** (engine) + a small per-weapon **speed-loss-per-pierce** (tuning). Let me fold that into the plan.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-29 08:54 UTC

Plan updated. To recap the decision on the piercing round:

- **Restitution alone is insufficient** — `min(0, asteroid)` already kills the rebound, but the normal impulse still stops the round at the surface. So we do add a parameter, just not on the `WeaponProfile`.
- **The parameter is `Collider.Sensor`** (engine): a sensor still fires `CollisionEvent`s (so fracture happens) but `CollisionSystem` skips overlap-separation and impulse for it — true pass-through. Implemented as an early-out in `GatherContact`/the resolve step, keeping the `Publish`.
- **`ProjectileSystem`** then owns the feel: fracture target + reciprocal self-fracture (the real stop mechanism — heavy/tough asteroids shatter the round fast) + a small per-weapon **speed-loss-per-pierce** knob for tuning + the existing lateral clamp.
- Side benefit: as a sensor, the round gets no impulse, so its velocity isn't mangled before fracture — sidestepping the Task 0 energy bug for the round specifically.

This is folded into Task 3 (with the `CollisionSystem` early-out spelled out) and referenced from Task 0's `ProjectileSystem`.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-29 08:59 UTC

Let me look at both constants and how they're used before giving an opinion.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-29 09:00 UTC

Good one to scrutinize. Having read both, here's my honest take — and they're not the same risk profile:

**`MaxCorrection` (8px cap on positional push/frame): I'd keep it.** This isn't really a band-aid — it's the standard Baumgarte clamp every rigid-body solver has (Box2D's `b2_maxLinearCorrection`). `CorrectionPercent = 0.4` already makes separation gentle, but `MaxCorrection` is the safety cap on top: without it, a *single* deep overlap from **any** source — a fast asteroid that tunneled a frame, a fragment that detached embedded, two concave crater walls interpenetrating — gets shoved `0.4 × depth` in one frame and **teleport-flings/spins**. That failure mode is independent of whatever the original bug was, and this game has lots of fast bodies + concave craters + fragments. Cheap insurance; removing it re-opens a whole class of blow-ups.

**`DeepNoBounce` (skip restitution past 6px overlap): defensible to remove, low stakes either way.** This one *is* the more situational guard — "don't let restitution launch an already-embedded body." If the real root (fragments spawning inside each other / the parent) is genuinely fixed — and it largely is, via the `FractureGroup`-sibling suppression in `TrySeparate` plus `DetachCellScale` shrinking lone cells — then this rarely fires, because `RestitutionVelThreshold = 30` already kills bounce for slow approaches. The only case it still catches is a body that's **both deeply embedded and approaching fast** (e.g. a high-speed tunnel). With piercing rounds becoming sensors (Task 3), the main fast-embedder is gone, so removing it is reasonable.

**My recommendation:** don't blanket-delete both. Keep `MaxCorrection` (it's legitimate solver hygiene, not a hack). Removing `DeepNoBounce` is fine **as a playtested experiment** once Task 0/3 land — pull it, then watch for embedded bodies launching on spawn/tunnel; if it's stable, it stays gone. If you want them both gone for cleanliness, I'd at least raise `MaxCorrection` to something generous (~20-30px) rather than remove it, so it only catches pathological overlaps.

Let me add that to the plan as a discrete item.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-29 09:00 UTC

Added as **Task 13**. Net of my recommendation, in the plan:

- **`MaxCorrection`** → keep (standard Baumgarte clamp; guards teleport-fling from any deep overlap, not just the old bug). If you want it gone for cleanliness, raise it to ~20–30px instead of deleting.
- **`DeepNoBounce`** → remove as a *playtested experiment* after Task 0/3 land (the spawn-inside root is now covered by `FractureGroup` sibling-suppression + `DetachCellScale`, `RestitutionVelThreshold` gates slow hits, and sensor piercing removes the main fast-embedder) — pull it, watch for launch-on-spawn/tunnel, revert if it regresses.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-29 10:14 UTC

Found it — and it's a real bug, not tuning. The piercing fracture uses **`pcfg.Mass` (the weapon's config `mass`, fixed at 50)** as the impactor mass, **not the round's actual density-derived RigidBody mass**. So when you 10× the `piercing_material` density, the round's *physics* mass changes (hence it bounces differently) but the **fracture energy doesn't move** — `mEff ≈ pcfg.Mass = 50` regardless of density, so `E` is pinned (~80k).

The fix: treat it like the asteroid-on-asteroid path — each side's impactor is the **other body's real `RigidBody.Mass`**. Then `E ∝` the round's real mass (density·area), and the reduced-mass keeps the round's self-fracture sane.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-06-30 10:26 UTC

Built. That was the missing piece of 0a.

## Why `E` was pinned at ~80k

The piercing fracture passed **`pcfg.Mass` (the weapon config `mass = 50`)** as the impactor mass — a constant unrelated to the material. So `mEff = reduced_mass(50, asteroid) ≈ 50` *no matter what you set the density to*. Your density change moved the round's **RigidBody** mass (so it bounced differently) but never reached the energy formula.

## The fix

Piercing now works like asteroid-on-asteroid: each side's impactor is the **other body's real `RigidBody.Mass`** (which *is* `density × area`, set in `PiercingPrefab`). So:
- crack into target → impactor = the round's real mass → **`E ∝` round density·area**;
- round's self-fracture → impactor = the asteroid's mass; reduced-mass keeps it ≈ the round's small mass, so it doesn't self-vaporize.

Now 10× the `piercing_material` density should give ~10× `E` in your `Console.WriteLine` (until the round gets heavy enough to rival the asteroid's mass, where the reduced mass naturally saturates — that's correct physics).

Two caveats for this retest:
- The round **still physically bounces** — it's not a sensor yet (that's Task 3). This change only makes the *computed energy* correct/scaling; the pass-through behavior comes next.
- Your debug `Console.WriteLine("E = " + E)` is still in — handy for this check; I left it for you.

2. Key Technical Concepts:
   - ECS engine: World, structs in SparseSet, ref-based mutation, deferred destruction, ISystem.Update(world, dt), EventBus (deferred publish, flushed by EventFlushSystem).
   - Conservative fracture model: per-cell energy split into transmit/dump; perpendicular-breaking via per-bond wc (conduct = lerp(1, max(0,align)^alignExp, directionality)) and wd (damage = lerp(|align|, 1-|align|, breakPerp)); strength = energy-to-break; FractureTuning globals (EnergyScale, ReachMin/Max, VaporEff, BreakPerp, AlignExponent, etc.).
   - Contact-impulse energy: E = EnergyScale·0.5·m_eff·v_n²·(1−e²); m_eff includes impactor mass + body mass + rotational lever (r×n)²/I.
   - CollisionSystem phases: narrow-phase collect+separate+publish event (Phase 3), then iterative velocity solve / impulses (Phase 4). The deferred event bus means CollisionEvent handlers run POST-solve → the root cause of the energy bug.
   - SOLID/SRP separation of collision behaviors into systems.
   - Sensor/trigger collider concept (pass-through: detect + publish but no separation/impulse).
   - Build command (CWD resets each Bash call): `cd /home/sebastian/ADVANCED/workspace/GameEngine && dotnet build <proj> -v q --nologo`. Four projects: AsteroidsEngine.csproj, Gameplay/AsteroidsGameplay.csproj, Game/AsteroidsGame.csproj, Demos/AsteroidDemo/AsteroidDemo.csproj.
   - SDL needs a display; the user playtests (I build + reason). Plan file: /home/sebastian/.claude/plans/rippling-whistling-scroll.md.

3. Files and Code Sections:
   - **Engine/Destruction/FractureService.cs** (0a — DONE)
     - `BeginFracture` signature changed: `Vector2 impactorVelocity` → `float normalSpeed`. Dropped `bodyLinear` local (kept `bodyAngular`). Kick: `Vector2 kick = dir * (normalSpeed * weapon.Knockback);`.
     - `ComputeEnergy` rewritten to take `normalSpeed` (no impactorVel/bodyLinear): `return FractureTuning.EnergyScale * 0.5f * mEff * normalSpeed * normalSpeed * (1f - e * e);`. Deleted dead `ComputeEnergyDebug`.
     - NOTE: user added a debug `if (weapon.Directionality > 0.8f) Console.WriteLine("E = " + E);` (~line 68) — left in for their testing.
   - **Engine/Components/Collider.cs** (0b — DONE): added `public bool Sensor;` (with doc comment).
   - **Engine/Events/CollisionEvent.cs** (0b — DONE): `public record CollisionEvent(Entity EntityA, Entity EntityB, ContactInfo Contact, float ApproachSpeed = 0f);`
   - **Engine/Systems/CollisionSystem.cs** (0b — DONE): added `bool sensor = cA.Sensor || cB.Sensor;`, changed `if (ResolveOverlap)` → `if (ResolveOverlap && !sensor)`, publish now passes `approach` from new helper:
     ```csharp
     private static float ApproachNormalSpeed(World world, Entity a, Entity b, Vector2 posA, Vector2 posB, in ContactInfo c)
     {
         Vector2 vA = world.HasComponent<Velocity>(a) ? VelAt(world.GetComponent<Velocity>(a), c.ContactPoint - posA) : Vector2.Zero;
         Vector2 vB = world.HasComponent<Velocity>(b) ? VelAt(world.GetComponent<Velocity>(b), c.ContactPoint - posB) : Vector2.Zero;
         return MathF.Max(0f, -Vector2.Dot(vA - vB, c.Normal));
     }
     ```
     - User-modified constants (left intact per instruction): `MaxCorrection = 16f`, `DeepNoBounce = 100000f` (Task 13 done by user).
   - **Gameplay/FractureGameplay.cs** (0a + 0c — DONE):
     - OnBulletHit: `bulletVel.Length()` passed as normalSpeed.
     - OnCollision: removed grenade-contact block and piercing block; added guard `if (_world.HasComponent<PiercingRoundTag>(eA) || _world.HasComponent<PiercingRoundTag>(eB)) return;` before the aIsBody/bIsBody guard; body-body now uses `float approach = ev.ApproachSpeed;`, `Vector2 n = ev.Contact.Normal;`, and `BeginFracture(_world, eA, -1, cp, n, approach, massBForA, weapon, _rng)` / `... -n ... massAForB ...`.
     - Still subscribes OnGrenadeDetonate (shrapnel spawn stays here). _activeCollisions still owned here (cleared in OnFractureCompleted/OnFractureSplit lines ~336/394). `Player` property + `IsDashInvincible()` here.
   - **Gameplay/Systems/GameSystems.cs** (0c — DONE):
     - GrenadeSystem ctor `(World world, EventBus bus)`, subscribes CollisionEvent→OnContact (detonate grenade on contact).
     - NEW `ProjectileSystem` class: `(World world, GameContext ctx, EventBus bus, Random rng, Func<Entity> player)`, subscribes CollisionEvent→OnContact; handles piercing (real masses: `roundMass`/`targetMass` from RigidBody.Mass, `speed = ev.ApproachSpeed`, `intoTarget = target == _player() ? roundMass * PlayerImpactCoeff : roundMass`), then lateral clamp.
     - AlienAiSystem (relevant for Task 1, NOT yet modified): `AlivePropellerFraction` line ~666 `return total == 0 ? 1f : (float)alive / total;` (the `1f` is the bug); `AliveWeaponFraction` ~683 `return total == 0 ? 0f : ...` (correct); `DroneSteering` ~598; bruiser uses direct pursuit; `FireAlienWeapon` ~699; uses ShootCooldown component + cfg.DetectionRadius (firing range).
