## 4. The dormant WinForms/GDI+ backend — `/home/sebastian/ADVANCED/workspace/GameEngine/Engine/Rendering/GameWindow.cs`

Entire file is wrapped in `#if WINDOWS ... #endif` (line 1 / 141). It is `Compile Remove`d from the engine (`AsteroidsEngine.csproj:30`), and the `WINDOWS` constant is **never defined in any `.csproj`** (no `DefineConstants` anywhere), so it currently compiles nowhere.

**What it implements:** `public sealed class GameWindow : Form` (line 21). It is a WinForms `Form` — it does **NOT** implement `IGameWindow`, and it does **NOT** implement `IRenderer` or `IPostEffects`. This is the pre-PAL design.

**Constructor** `GameWindow(int width, int height, InputSystem input, GameLoop loop)` (34): sets `Text = "Asteroids"`, `ClientSize`, `FixedSingle` border, no maximize, center screen; enables `ControlStyles.UserPaint | AllPaintingInWmPaint | OptimizedDoubleBuffer`; allocates two `Bitmap` buffers; wires `_loop.OnDraw = DrawFrame`; wires input; wires `FormClosing` to `_loop.Stop()`.

**GDI+ drawing / double-buffer / present:**
- Two `Bitmap` fields `_backBuffer` / `_frontBuffer` (28-29).
- `DrawFrame()` (78): `using var g = Graphics.FromImage(_backBuffer)`; sets `SmoothingMode.AntiAlias`, `InterpolationMode.NearestNeighbor`, `g.Clear(Color.Black)`; invokes `OnGameDraw?.Invoke(g)`; then atomically swaps via `Interlocked.Exchange(ref _frontBuffer, _backBuffer)` and calls `Invalidate()`.
- Rendering is exposed as `public Action&lt;Graphics&gt;? OnGameDraw { get; set; }` (102) — i.e. it hands out a raw `System.Drawing.Graphics`; there is **no `IRenderer` here at all**.
- `OnPaint(PaintEventArgs e)` (108): `Volatile.Read(ref _frontBuffer)` then `e.Graphics.DrawImage(bmp, 0, 0)`.
- `OnPaintBackground` overridden empty (117) to suppress erase.
- `Dispose(bool)` (131) disposes both bitmaps.

**Key mapping / input forwarding** (64-71):

```csharp
KeyDown   += (_, e) =&gt; _input.OnKeyDown((KeyCode)(int)e.KeyCode);
KeyUp     += (_, e) =&gt; _input.OnKeyUp((KeyCode)(int)e.KeyCode);
MouseMove += (_, e) =&gt; _input.OnMouseMove(e.Location);
MouseDown += (_, e) =&gt; _input.OnMouseButton(WinToEngine(e.Button), pressed: true);
MouseUp   += (_, e) =&gt; _input.OnMouseButton(WinToEngine(e.Button), pressed: false);
FormClosing += (_, _) =&gt; _loop.Stop();
```

Key mapping is a **direct cast** `(KeyCode)(int)e.KeyCode` (works because `KeyCode` values equal `Forms.Keys`). Mouse mapping via `WinToEngine(MouseButtons)` (123) → `MouseButton.Left/Right/Middle`, default Left. Note `MouseMove` passes `e.Location` (a `System.Drawing.Point`) directly to `OnMouseMove(Vector2)` — relies on an implicit conversion that does not exist for `Point`→`Vector2`; this would not compile as-is against the current `InputSystem` signature (`Vector2`).

**What is incomplete / stale relative to CURRENT interfaces:**
- Does not implement `IGameWindow`: missing `Width`/`Height` (it exposes `GameWidth`/`GameHeight` instead, 31-32), `ShouldClose`, `IRenderer Renderer`, `PollEvents()`, `Present()`, and the five events (`KeyDown`/`KeyUp`/`MouseMoved`/`MouseButtonChanged`/`TextInput`). Instead it forwards directly into a captured `InputSystem` and uses `GameLoop.OnDraw`/`FormClosing`.
- No `IRenderer` implementation — it hands out `Graphics` via `OnGameDraw`, whereas the engine now draws exclusively through `IRenderer`. There is no `DrawLine/DrawPolygon/FillPath/DrawText/MeasureText`, no transform stack, no `FontSpec`/`Color` conversion, no `IPostEffects.Distort`.
- No `TextInput` support (predates the `TextInput`/`OnTextInput` text path).
- Couples to `GameLoop` and `InputSystem` in its constructor (push model), whereas the current PAL is pull-based (`PollEvents`/`Present`) with app-level event wiring.
- `MouseMove` `Point`→`Vector2` mismatch noted above.
- Its `Color.Black` refers to `System.Drawing.Color`, not the engine `Color`.

**Other GDI+/WinForms files under `Engine/Rendering/`:** none. Grepping `Engine/Rendering/` for `Graphics|Bitmap|WinForms|Gdi|System.Drawing` matched only `GameWindow.cs` (real usage) plus `IRenderer.cs` and `Color.cs` (mentions in comments only). No `TwoPlayerDemo.cs`, no `*Gdi*` file exists on disk anywhere (referenced only historically in the csproj comment at `AsteroidsEngine.csproj:15-16`).

## 🧑 User · 2026-07-20 14:14 UTC

How do you think the Winforms backend runs compared to the SDL one performance-wise?

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-20 14:15 UTC

Honest answer up front: I'd expect the WinForms/GDI+ backend to be **meaningfully slower under load — likely a lot slower in the worst scenes** — because it's a fundamentally different rendering model. This is reasoned, not measured (I can't benchmark it here), but the architecture makes the direction clear.

## Why — the core difference
- **SDL backend = GPU raster.** SkiaSharp runs on a `GRContext.CreateGl` surface, so every polygon fill, circle, and the AA are rasterized on the **GPU**, and `Present` is a GL buffer flip. The CPU just records draw calls.
- **WinForms backend = CPU software raster.** GDI+ (`System.Drawing.Graphics`) rasterizes everything on the **CPU**, single-threaded, into a system-memory `Bitmap`, and `Present` is a full-screen CPU→GDI blit. The sim and the rendering now compete for the same cores.

For this game specifically, that gap bites hard because the frame is **fill- and primitive-heavy**:

## Where it'll hurt most
1. **Dense fracture scenes.** Asteroids draw a per-cell `FillPolygon` (+ an underlay `FillPath`) — hundreds to low-thousands of *antialiased* convex fills per frame. `SmoothingMode.AntiAlias` in GDI+ is expensive per-primitive on CPU; on the GPU it's nearly free. This is the dominant factor.
2. **Particle storms (grenades / dust).** `ParticleSystem` draws each particle as a `FillCircle`. A grenade puts hundreds–thousands of AA ellipses on screen for a few frames. That's exactly the scenario already flagged for the deferred grenade-FPS task — and it'll be *worse* on GDI+. (Note the deferred optimization should be profiled **per backend**.)
3. **The warp (`Distort`).** Skia does it in ~one GPU snapshot + one `DrawVertices`. My GDI+ version clones a ~1240px region to a `Bitmap` and does `gridX×gridY` bilinear `DrawImage` blits *every frame the vortex eye or a border is on screen* — up to ~400 CPU warps for the eye alone, plus border strips. This is the single most expensive thing in the WinForms path and can spike frame time near the vortex/edges.
4. **Fixed per-frame costs SDL doesn't pay.** A full-screen ~8 MB `DrawImageUnscaled` blit every frame (at 1080p, ~1 GB/s at 120 fps), plus DWM composition (borderless fullscreen doesn't bypass the compositor), plus per-call P/Invoke overhead across thousands of GDI+ draw calls.

## Where it'll be fine
Menus, the demo-reel background, and light scenes (a handful of bodies) will run comfortably — GDI+ is perfectly good for low primitive counts. The `Update`/simulation cost is identical either way. It's purely the *raster* side that diverges.

## Net expectation
The 120 Hz soft cap means it simply **won't reach the cap under load** — frame time balloons instead. I'd guess it holds up in calm play and drops well below 60 in dense fracture / grenade / warp-active moments at native 1080p fullscreen. Playable, not smooth at peak.

## Levers if it's too slow on Windows (cheapest first)
- **Render to a smaller back-buffer and scale up on blit** — biggest win; software raster cost is ~linear in pixels, and native 1080p+ is punishing.
- **Drop AA under load** (`SmoothingMode.HighSpeed`) — large gain, modest quality hit.
- **Cap/skip the warp** — it's feature-detected, so it can be disabled entirely on GDI+ with no other change; or keep the grid tiny.
- **Cheaper particles** (cap counts, or draw as rects not AA ellipses).
- Two implementation nits I can tighten now: `GdiRenderer.FillPath` allocates a `PointF[]` per contour per frame (GC churn — should reuse a buffer), and `Present` calls `CreateGraphics()` each frame (cache it). Minor vs raster cost, but free wins.

The trade you're buying with this backend is **zero native dependencies / trivial Windows deployment**, at the cost of peak framerate. If performance matters on Windows, the SDL+Skia exe also runs there (Skia has a Windows native package) — WinForms is the "just works, no deps" option, not the fast one.

Want me to apply those two cheap `GdiRenderer` fixes (reuse the `FillPath` buffer, cache the present `Graphics`) while it's fresh, even though we can't measure them here?

## 🧑 User · 2026-07-20 14:21 UTC

Does GDI not support GLU rastering? What's rastering anyway, how does this work

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-20 14:22 UTC

Good questions — and a useful distinction to nail down. (Quick note: "GLU" is the *OpenGL Utility Library*, an old helper on top of OpenGL — you almost certainly mean **GPU** rasterization / hardware acceleration. I'll answer that.)

## What "rasterization" is

Everything the game draws starts as **vector** descriptions — pure geometry + color: "a convex polygon with *these* corner points, filled with *this* RGBA," "a line from A to B, 2px wide," "a circle at C radius r." That's a mathematical shape, not pixels.

Your screen is a **raster**: a fixed grid of pixels (e.g. 1920×1080). **Rasterization is the step that converts a vector shape into the specific pixels that represent it** — deciding, for every pixel the shape touches, *whether* it's covered and *what color* to write into the image buffer.

Concretely, to fill one polygon a rasterizer does roughly this:
1. Find the polygon's bounding rows (scanlines).
2. For each scanline, compute where the polygon's edges cross it → that gives horizontal spans "inside" the shape (using an inside/outside rule — even-odd or nonzero winding).
3. Write the fill color into every pixel in those spans.

```
edges →   /\          scanline y:  find crossings  →  ███████  fill the span
         /  \                       x=3 .. x=11
        /____\
```

**Antialiasing** is the expensive refinement: a pixel that an edge only *partially* covers (say 40%) shouldn't be fully on or fully off — it gets a blended color weighted by coverage, so edges look smooth instead of stair-stepped. Computing that per-edge coverage is real work, multiplied across every edge pixel of every shape.

Then the finished pixel buffer is **presented** (shown on screen).

## Where the rasterizing runs — CPU vs GPU

This is the whole difference between the two backends:

- **Software rasterization (CPU).** A loop, in ordinary CPU code, walks scanlines/pixels and writes colors into a memory buffer — serial, one span at a time, AA computed by hand in that loop. Flexible and dependency-free, but bounded by single-thread CPU throughput. **This is GDI+.**
- **Hardware rasterization (GPU).** The GPU has dedicated, *massively parallel* rasterizer hardware built for exactly one thing: filling triangles with coverage/AA, sampling textures, and blending — thousands of pixels at once. You hand it geometry (triangles) and small programs (shaders); it fills the framebuffer. **This is Skia-on-OpenGL, i.e. the SDL backend.**

A GPU is essentially a purpose-built rasterization engine. "Fill lots of antialiased polygons and blend them" is its native workload — which is precisely what a dense fracture scene or a particle storm is. A CPU does the same job correctly but one span at a time, so it falls behind under that load.

## So — does GDI+ support GPU rasterization?

**No. GDI+ is a software (CPU) rasterizer by design; there is no GPU path in it.** A bit of nuance on the family:

- Old **GDI** (not +) *used* to get some driver acceleration for simple ops (BitBlt, solid rects/lines) back in the XP era. Since Windows Vista's WDDM driver model + the DWM compositor, most of that went away; GDI now renders to a system-memory surface that DWM composites, and only a few blit fast-paths stay accelerated.
- **GDI+** — the antialiased vector API we actually use (`System.Drawing.Graphics`) — has **always been pure CPU**. Antialiased paths, alpha blending, image transforms: all software.

If you want **GPU-accelerated 2D on Windows**, GDI+ isn't the tool — you'd use one of:
- **Direct2D** (Microsoft's GPU 2D API, sits on Direct3D/DXGI),
- a **GL/Vulkan-backed library like Skia** — which is exactly what the SDL backend already does, and Skia *also* has Windows GL/Direct3D backends,
- or **WPF** (retained-mode, Direct3D-backed).

That's the trade the WinForms backend makes: GDI+ buys you **zero native dependencies and trivial Windows deployment**, at the cost of being CPU-bound — so it's the "just works everywhere on Windows, no libs" option, not the fast one. If Windows performance mattered, the cleaner answer would be to run the existing **Skia** renderer on Windows (GPU) rather than write a GDI+ one — the GDI+ backend's real value is portability/simplicity, not speed.

## 🧑 User · 2026-07-20 14:45 UTC

So the stack is a rasterizer + an ui renderer, and the latter is never the bottleneck since it just presents the frame, the costly part is actually drawing it? Can you explain how are the responsibilites between renderer and the rasterizer split?

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-20 14:46 UTC

Good instinct, but the split is a bit finer than "rasterizer + presenter" — there are really **four** layers, and the word "renderer" is overloaded, which is what's tangling it. Let me map them to the actual code.

## The four layers, per frame

**1. Scene / draw-issuing — the game's own render code** (`WorldRenderer`, the HUD in `PlayingState`)
This decides *what* to draw. It loops over entities, transforms each cell's vertices into screen space, picks colors, and emits immediate-mode calls: `r.FillPolygon(verts, color)`, `r.DrawLine(...)`, `r.DrawText(...)`. Pure CPU; cost scales with **how many objects / draw calls** you issue.

**2. The renderer backend — the `IRenderer` implementation** (`SkiaRenderer` / `GdiRenderer`)
This is the *drawing API*, not the presenter. It's a thin **adapter**: it translates each engine call into the underlying library's call — `FillPolygon(span, Color)` → `canvas.DrawPath(...)` (Skia) or `graphics.FillPolygon(...)` (GDI+). It builds the native path/point objects and hands them down. Cost: per-call interop overhead. It does **not** itself fill pixels.

**3. The rasterizer — the graphics library behind the backend** (Skia, or GDI+ itself)
This is where vectors become pixels: coverage tests, antialiasing, blending into the target buffer. **This is the expensive part**, and it's CPU (GDI+) or GPU (Skia). "Drawing it," in your phrasing.

**4. Presentation — the window** (`IGameWindow.Present`)
Takes the finished pixel buffer and puts it on screen: a GL buffer-swap (SDL/Skia) or a full-screen blit of the bitmap to the form (WinForms). Cost: fixed per frame, usually cheap.

So your instinct is right that **presentation (layer 4) is rarely the bottleneck** — but "the renderer" you were picturing is layers 2+3, and *rasterization (3) is the costly one*, not presentation.

## Responsibility split, crisply

| Layer | Owns | Answers | Cost driver |
|---|---|---|---|
| Game render code | scene traversal | *what* shapes exist | # of draw calls |
| `IRenderer` backend | API translation | *how to say it* to Skia/GDI+ | per-call overhead |
| Rasterizer (Skia/GDI+) | pixels | *which pixels, what color* | fill rate × AA × blends |
| `IGameWindow.Present` | display | *show the buffer* | one flip / blit |

## Two subtleties that matter

**"Drawing" is actually two separable costs.** Even when rasterization is free (GPU), you can be **draw-call bound**: if the game issues thousands of tiny `FillPolygon` calls (our per-cell rendering does exactly this), the CPU spends all its time *submitting* draws and the GPU sits idle waiting. So layer 1+2 (CPU draw submission) and layer 3 (fill) are independent bottlenecks — a scene can be limited by either. Our per-cell fracture rendering is draw-call-heavy *and* fill-heavy.

**Where the cost *appears* differs between backends.** GDI+ is **immediate**: each `FillPolygon` rasterizes into the bitmap synchronously, so the pixel cost is spread across the draw calls themselves. Skia on GL often **records** the canvas ops and the GPU rasterizes them in a batch at `Flush()`/`Present()` time — so in a profiler the fill cost shows up at *present*, not at each draw call. Same total work, different place on the timeline. (That's why "present is cheap" is true for GDI+ but can be misleading for a GPU backend — the swap is cheap, but the flush that precedes it isn't.)

So to answer directly: **the renderer (`IRenderer`) issues draws; the rasterizer (Skia/GDI+) turns them into pixels; the window presents.** Presentation is cheap. The cost is "drawing," and that cost splits into *submitting* the draws (CPU, layers 1–2) and *filling* the pixels (layer 3, CPU on GDI+ / GPU on Skia) — which of those two dominates depends on whether you're throwing lots of *calls* or lots of *covered pixels* at it. Our game does both, which is why it's the demanding case for the CPU-only GDI+ path.

## 🧑 User · 2026-07-20 15:21 UTC

Can Winforms use Skia as the rasterizer? What other responsibilities does the IGameWindow have (like input handling, audio etc.?)

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-20 15:22 UTC

Two separate things, and the answer to the first reframes the whole perf discussion.

## Q1 — Can WinForms use Skia as the rasterizer?

**Yes.** "WinForms" and "GDI+" are two independent choices that I happened to bundle. WinForms is just the *window shell* (create window, pump input, present); the rasterizer behind the `IRenderer` can be anything. Three real combinations:

| Window | Rasterizer | Deps | Speed |
|---|---|---|---|
| WinForms | **GDI+** (what I built) | none | CPU, slowest |
| WinForms | **Skia — CPU** | SkiaSharp | CPU, but a *much* better CPU rasterizer than GDI+ (SIMD, higher quality AA) |
| WinForms | **Skia — GPU** | SkiaSharp + a GL context | GPU, ≈ the SDL backend |

The tooling for this already exists: the **`SkiaSharp.Views.WindowsForms`** package gives you `SKControl` (Skia rendering into a bitmap that WinForms blits — CPU) and `SKGLControl` (hosts an OpenGL context *inside* a WinForms control and hands you a GPU-backed `SKSurface` — hardware raster).

And here's the nice part for *our* architecture: our `SkiaRenderer` is constructed from an **`SKSurface`** and is otherwise surface-agnostic (even `Distort`'s snapshot works on any `SKSurface`). So a "WinForms + Skia" backend wouldn't need a new renderer at all — the `WinFormsGameWindow` would host a Skia surface (via `SKGLControl` for GPU or an `SKBitmap` surface for CPU) and simply return the **existing `SkiaRenderer`** as its `Renderer`. WinForms does window+input+present; Skia does raster; zero renderer duplication.

This is exactly why the PAL splits `IGameWindow` from `IRenderer`: the two are orthogonal. So circling back to the performance question — **if Windows speed were the goal, the right move is a WinForms (or SDL) window + Skia-GPU, not a hand-written GDI+ renderer.** The GDI+ backend's *only* merit is "no native dependencies at all." Skia-on-WinForms costs you the SkiaSharp dependency (and, for GPU, a GL context) but gets you back to hardware rasterization inside a plain WinForms app.

## Q2 — What else does `IGameWindow` own?

From the actual interface, its responsibilities are narrow and specific:

1. **Window lifecycle** — open the OS window (ctor), report `Width`/`Height`, signal `ShouldClose`, clean up (`IDisposable`).
2. **Input *source*** — `PollEvents()` drains the OS event queue and raises five raw events: `KeyDown`/`KeyUp`, `MouseMoved`, `MouseButtonChanged`, `TextInput`.
3. **Renderer access** — expose the `IRenderer` bound to its surface.
4. **Presentation** — `Present()` shows the finished frame.

Note input is **source, not logic**. The window only forwards *raw OS events* (this key went down, the mouse is here). It has no notion of "held," "pressed this frame," or edge detection — that's `InputSystem` in the game layer (`BeginFrame`, `IsPressed`, `ConsumePress`, suppression). Input lives on the window for a concrete reason: on every platform, OS input arrives *through the window's message queue* (WinForms message pump, SDL event queue), so the window is the natural receiver; `GameHost` wires those events into `InputSystem`.

**Audio is *not* on `IGameWindow`** — and shouldn't be. There's no audio in the project today; the engine's PAL comments mention a future **`IAudioBackend`** as a *separate* interface, constructed independently. That's deliberate: audio doesn't come through the window's message queue and doesn't touch the drawing surface, so bolting it onto the window would violate the one-concern-per-interface split. Same for anything else people sometimes cram into a "window" class:

- **Input interpretation / state** → `InputSystem` (game layer).
- **The loop & timing** → `GameHost` (the window is passive; it's *called*, it doesn't drive).
- **Asset/resource loading** → `GameConfigLoader` now, a future `IResourceManager` for images.
- **Rasterization** → the `IRenderer`'s backing library.
- **Audio (future)** → its own `IAudioBackend`.

So the clean one-liner: **`IGameWindow` is "the OS surface" — open it, pump its raw events, show pixels on it, close it.** It's the input *source* and the present target, and nothing else. Everything that isn't intrinsically tied to that OS window is a separate PAL interface or a game-layer service.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-20 19:41 UTC

`|` (not `||`) is deliberate — both operands evaluate, so the latch is always consumed even when held-fire is active. Let me build:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-20 19:41 UTC

Builds clean. Here's the diagnosis and what I changed — they're two unrelated problems.

## Bullets sometimes don't spawn — **software bug, fixed**

The cannon fired on `inp.IsMouseLeft`, which is **held-state only** — there was no press-edge latch for the mouse. Combined with the loop structure, a click could be dropped two ways:

1. **Batch-coalesced tap:** `PollEvents()` drains *all* pending OS events at once. A fast click delivers `down` then `up` in the same batch → `_mouseLeftPending` goes true then false → committed reads **false** → the click never happened as far as gameplay is concerned.
2. **0-step frame:** `FixedTimestep.Advance` returns **0** on some frames (timing jitter from `Thread.Sleep` granularity). If the "button held" window lands entirely on frames that run no `Update`, no fixed step ever observes it.

Both are intermittent → "sometimes."

The fix: a **sticky click latch** in `InputSystem` — set on button-*down*, cleared only when a game step **consumes** it (`ConsumeMouseLeftClick()`), not per render frame. So it survives both a sub-frame tap and any number of 0-step frames until an `Update` actually reads it. The cannon/shotgun now fire on `ConsumeMouseLeftClick() | IsMouseLeft` (bitwise `|` so the consume always runs — held-fire still works, and there's no phantom shot after release). One bullet per tap, exactly.

## >3 keys stop working — **almost certainly your keyboard, not the code**

That a *count* threshold (~3) breaks it is the classic signature of **keyboard ghosting / limited n-key rollover** — a hardware property of most membrane keyboards. The key matrix physically can't report certain 3+ simultaneous presses, so SDL never receives those events and the game can't know a key is down. Nothing in the code caps it: `InputSystem` uses an unbounded `HashSet`, and mouse is a separate USB device (which is why mouse-aim keeps working while WASD+skill drops).

To confirm it's hardware: open an online **key-rollover / ghosting tester** and mash W+A+S+Space+Q — you'll see the extra keys fail to register in the browser too, with no game involved. A different keyboard (or one advertising "N-key rollover / anti-ghosting") won't have the limit. WASD are clustered, which is a common ghosting hotspot; if you want a marginal improvement without new hardware, remapping thrust to a more rollover-friendly spread (e.g. arrows) sometimes helps, but the real fix is an NKRO keyboard.

I built it but **can't runtime-test input here** (SDL needs a display). Worth a quick playtest: single fast clicks should now always fire, and holding should be unchanged. If you *also* see skill keys (Q/E/R) occasionally drop on a single tap, that's the same 0-step/edge issue on the keyboard path — say the word and I'll extend the same latch to key presses.

## 🧑 User · 2026-07-21 14:35 UTC

<ide_opened_file>The user opened the file /home/sebastian/ADVANCED/workspace/GameEngine/Gameplay/WeaponEffects.cs in the IDE. This may or may not be related to the current task.</ide_opened_file>
I actually found the bug myself (it's a bug I myself introduced trying to solve a problem). What happens when a bullet spawns inside an entity and gets immediately consumed?

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-21 14:37 UTC

Right — here's the exact mechanism, frame by frame, for a bullet that spawns inside an entity **E**:

1. `PlayerControlSystem` (a fixed step) spawns the bullet at the muzzle — which is *inside E's collider* — with `Prev = Pos = muzzle`, and sets the cannon cooldown. **Cooldown is now reset.**
2. `MovementSystem` advances it: `Pos = muzzle + v·dt` (`Prev` still `muzzle`).
3. `RaycastBulletSystem` casts `from = muzzle` (inside E) → `to`. A ray whose *origin is already inside* E's polygon reports a hit (the near edge / exit), so it publishes `BulletHitEvent(E, bullet, …)`.
4. `EventFlushSystem` → `OnBulletHit`: `BeginFracture(E, …)` **and** `DestroyEntity(bullet)`.
5. `FlushDeferred` at end of frame removes the bullet — **before `Draw` ever runs.**

So three things happen, and only the first is the visible symptom:

- **No projectile.** The bullet is destroyed the same frame it's born, so cooldown resets but nothing flies. Exactly "sometimes no bullet."
- **E takes a point-blank fracture** with no visible shot. If E is an asteroid you're overlapping, it's silent free damage; **if E is your own ship, you fracture yourself.**
- **The shot's energy is dumped at the muzzle**, not downrange.

The thing that's *supposed* to prevent the self-case is **owner-grace**: for `OwnerGrace` seconds, `RaycastBulletSystem` does `if (inGrace && hit.Entity == owner) continue;` — it skips the hit entirely, so the bullet is **not** consumed and survives to exit the hull. That only works when `hit.Entity == owner`. It does nothing when the bullet spawns inside a *different* body (an asteroid the ship is touching), or when `owner` no longer matches the ship it's overlapping — e.g. right after a **cockpit transfer**, the in-flight bullet's `owner` is the old (now-dead) player entity, so its hit on the *new* player fragment isn't recognized as a self-hit → it shoots and consumes itself against your own new ship.

That's why bumping `BulletGrace` to `1.0f` "fixes" it but is a band-aid: it widens the self-ignore window, but (a) it only helps the *owner* case, not overlapped asteroids, (b) it lets your bullets pass harmlessly *through you* for a full second, and (c) it doesn't survive an owner-identity change mid-flight.

The robust fixes, depending on what you were originally solving:
- **Spawn the bullet outside the hull** — offset the muzzle past the ship's bounding radius so it never originates inside its own collider. Kills the self-case at the source; grace can go back to ~0.1s just for edge cases.
- **Skip the first segment's raycast** for a freshly-spawned bullet (a one-frame spawn grace on *all* layers), so every bullet is guaranteed at least one visible step of travel before it can be consumed.
- **Track owner across transfer** (carry the owner as the *fracture group* / ship lineage rather than the exact entity id) so grace still applies after a cockpit swap.

Which problem were you originally trying to solve when you introduced it? If you tell me, I'll wire the right one of those in rather than leave the 1.0s grace — my hunch is "spawn outside the hull + short grace" is the clean answer, but it depends on why the muzzle ended up inside the ship.

## Tier 1 — the real gaps that make it feel unfinished

**1. Audio (the biggest one).** There is *no sound at all* — no weapon fire, no fracture crunch, no impact thump, no boss cue, no music. **2. Performance — and it just got urgent.** You bumped `grenade.shrapnelCount` to **260** (from 45). On top of it, the vortex/rim **warp snapshots a big region every frame**. This is the top technical debt. Do them in this order: **profile + fix the grenade/warp performance first** (it's the most broken thing right now and blocks enjoyable playtesting of everything else), then **audio** (the biggest felt gap), then **menu/controls polish + balance pass**, then **packaging + the Windows verification**.

# GameEngine — Cross-platform packaging + WinForms-on-Skia + profiling reconnaissance

## 1. SkiaRenderer reuse

**File:** `/home/sebastian/ADVANCED/workspace/GameEngine/Platform/Sdl/SkiaRenderer.cs`

The renderer is **surface-agnostic**. It takes an `SKSurface` (+ width/height) and does all drawing through `surface.Canvas`. There is **zero** GL / SDL / `GRContext` reference anywhere in this file — it only depends on `SkiaSharp` and the engine's `IRenderer`/`IPostEffects` interfaces (imports at lines 1-3). A WinForms host could construct the identical renderer over a different `SKSurface` (GPU-backed via an `SKGLControl`, or a CPU raster surface).

Constructor (lines 26-32) and fields (lines 14-16):
```csharp
private readonly SKSurface _surface;
private readonly SKCanvas _canvas;
private readonly int _width, _height;
...
public SkiaRenderer(SKSurface surface, int width, int height)
{
    _surface = surface;
    _canvas  = surface.Canvas;
    _width   = width;
    _height  = height;
}
```

The `Distort` post-effect snapshot (lines 156-162) depends **only** on the `SKSurface`, not on GL:
```csharp
// Snapshot the region as it stands, so the warp resamples the frame drawn so far.
_canvas.Flush();
using var image = _surface.Snapshot(new SKRectI(x0, y0, x1, y1));
if (image == null) return;
...
using var shader = SKShader.CreateImage(image, SKShaderTileMode.Clamp, SKShaderTileMode.Clamp);
```
It uses `_surface.Snapshot(...)` → `SKShader.CreateImage` → `_canvas.DrawVertices(...)` (line 194). All portable Skia calls; works identically over a raster or GL surface. Comment at line 115 of `SdlGameWindow.cs` confirms the surface is passed in *"because it needs the surface for snapshot-based post FX"*.

**Current GDI+ backend that Skia would replace:**
- `/home/sebastian/ADVANCED/workspace/GameEngine/Platform/WinForms/WinFormsGameWindow.cs` — a `Form` implementing `IGameWindow`. Owns a back-buffer `Bitmap` + `Graphics` (lines 46-48), constructs a `GdiRenderer` (line 48). `Present()` (lines 93-97) blits the bitmap via `CreateGraphics().DrawImageUnscaled(_backBuffer, 0, 0)`. `PollEvents()` = `Application.DoEvents()` (line 91). No GL; pure GDI+ CPU raster + blit.
- `/home/sebastian/ADVANCED/workspace/GameEngine/Platform/WinForms/GdiRenderer.cs` — implements the same `IRenderer` + `IPostEffects` as `SkiaRenderer`, drawing into the back-buffer `Bitmap` via `System.Drawing`/GDI+. Its `Distort` (lines 134-183) approximates the mesh warp with per-cell affine `DrawImage`, snapshotting via `_backBuffer.Clone(...)` (line 147). Comment (line 16) explicitly says it *"Mirrors the structure of the SkiaSharp backend."*

## 2. How native graphics deps are referenced

Every `PackageReference` in the repo (only three csproj files carry any):

`/home/sebastian/ADVANCED/workspace/GameEngine/Platform/Sdl/Engine.Platform.Sdl.csproj` (lines 18-21):
```xml
&lt;ItemGroup&gt;
  &lt;PackageReference Include="Silk.NET.SDL" Version="2.21.0" /&gt;
  &lt;PackageReference Include="SkiaSharp" Version="2.88.7" /&gt;
&lt;/ItemGroup&gt;
```

`/home/sebastian/ADVANCED/workspace/GameEngine/Game/AsteroidsGame.csproj` (lines 18-20) — the SDL/Skia game exe:
```xml
&lt;ItemGroup&gt;
  &lt;PackageReference Include="SkiaSharp.NativeAssets.Linux.NoDependencies" Version="2.88.7" /&gt;
&lt;/ItemGroup&gt;
```

`/home/sebastian/ADVANCED/workspace/GameEngine/Demos/AsteroidDemo/AsteroidDemo.csproj` (lines 18-21):
```xml
&lt;ItemGroup&gt;
  &lt;!-- Runtime native Skia lib (managed Skia/Silk come transitively from the backend). --&gt;
  &lt;PackageReference Include="SkiaSharp.NativeAssets.Linux.NoDependencies" Version="2.88.7" /&gt;
&lt;/ItemGroup&gt;
```

Findings:
- **SkiaSharp** = `2.88.7` (managed, in the SDL backend). **Native Skia** = `SkiaSharp.NativeAssets.Linux.NoDependencies` `2.88.7`, added only by the two **executable** projects (game + demo). There is **no** `SkiaSharp.NativeAssets.Win32`, `SkiaSharp.NativeAssets.macOS`, or any `SkiaSharp.Views.*` package anywhere in the repo (grep returned nothing). So today Skia only ships its Linux native.
- **Silk.NET.SDL** = `2.21.0`. Silk.NET.SDL is a P/Invoke binding only — it does **not** bundle a native `libSDL2`. `SdlGameWindow` gets its API via `SdlApi.GetApi()` (line 61, `Silk.NET.SDL.Sdl.GetApi()`), which resolves a **system-installed libSDL2** at runtime. No SDL native-assets package is referenced, so the SDL2 shared library must be present on the host. Skia's GL entry points are likewise loaded from the **system** `libEGL.so.1` / `libGL.so.1` at runtime (`SdlGameWindow.CreateGlInterface`, lines 211-230), not from a package.
- **RID / self-contained / publish settings:** none. Grep for `RuntimeIdentifier(s)`, `SelfContained`, `PublishSingleFile`, `PublishTrimmed`, `InvariantGlobalization` across all csproj/props/targets returned **zero hits**. No `Directory.Build.props`, no `.sln`, no `nuget.config` (only auto-generated `obj/AsteroidsEngine.csproj.nuget.g.props`). All projects are framework-dependent, `net8.0`; the two WinForms projects are `net8.0-windows` with `&lt;UseWindowsForms&gt;true&lt;/UseWindowsForms&gt;` (WinForms csproj line 8; Game.WinForms csproj line 9).

TargetFrameworks: engine, gameplay, gamecore, SDL backend, game, demo = `net8.0`; both WinForms projects = `net8.0-windows`. The WinForms csproj comments (lines 4-6) stress they *"CANNOT build on Linux."*

## 3. How the SDL window creates its GL+Skia surface

**File:** `/home/sebastian/ADVANCED/workspace/GameEngine/Platform/Sdl/SdlGameWindow.cs`

This is the GPU/GL path a WinForms Skia host would need to replicate (WinForms option would be either an `SKGLControl` GL surface, or a CPU `SKSurface` blitted like the current GDI path). Surface-creation block (lines 98-116):
```csharp
// GRGlInterface.CreateOpenGl() uses GLX and crashes on Wayland/EGL contexts.
// eglGetProcAddress works for both desktop GL and GLES and covers both backends.
// Fall back to GLX (X11) if EGL is not available.
var glInterface = CreateGlInterface()
    ?? throw new InvalidOperationException("Could not create GRGlInterface (EGL/GLX)");

_grContext = GRContext.CreateGl(glInterface)
    ?? throw new InvalidOperationException("GRContext.CreateGl returned null");

// Describe the default framebuffer: id=0, internal format GL_RGBA8 (0x8058).
var fbInfo = new GRGlFramebufferInfo(0, 0x8058);
var rt     = new GRBackendRenderTarget(width, height, sampleCount: 0, stencilBits: 8, fbInfo);

// BottomLeft: OpenGL's origin is bottom-left; Skia's is top-left.
_surface = SKSurface.Create(_grContext, rt, GRSurfaceOrigin.BottomLeft, SKColorType.Rgba8888)
    ?? throw new InvalidOperationException("SKSurface.Create returned null");

_skiaRenderer = new SkiaRenderer(_surface, width, height);   // needs the surface for snapshot-based post FX
```
Preceding GL setup (lines 78-96): `GLSetAttribute` OpenGL 3.3 Core + `StencilSize 8` + `Doublebuffer 1` (Skia needs the stencil for path fills), `CreateWindow(WindowFlags.Opengl)`, `GLCreateContext`, `GLMakeCurrent`, `GLSetSwapInterval(0)` (vsync off; the loop caps frames). The header doc-comment (lines 12-24) restates the whole init sequence.

`Present()` (lines 164-169):
```csharp
public void Present()
{
    _surface.Canvas.Flush();
    _grContext.Flush();
    _sdl.GLSwapWindow(_window);
}
```
No pixel copy — Skia rasterizes directly on the GPU, then `SDL_GL_SwapWindow`. (Contrast the WinForms/GDI `Present()` in §1, which blits a CPU bitmap.) `Renderer =&gt; _skiaRenderer` (line 41). Disposal order (lines 241-255): renderer → surface → grContext → GL context → window.

## 4. Existing profiling / timing hooks

**Main loop:** `/home/sebastian/ADVANCED/workspace/GameEngine/GameCore/GameHost.cs`. Fixed timestep `1.0/120.0` (line 18). Uses one `Stopwatch` for frame timing (lines 43, 51-52, 77) and a `FixedTimestep` accumulator. It measures **whole-frame** time and a post-render `elapsed` for the soft frame cap (lines 77-79) — there is **no per-system timing and no FPS surfaced** in the shared GameHost. There is **no FPS display** in the shipped game (only the demo has one).

```csharp
foreach (var s in _systems)
{
    // Once the run is over the hulk just drifts — no more steering, thrust or firing.
    if (s is PlayerControlSystem pcs)
    {

_world.FlushDeferred();
_fx.Update((float)gameDt);
```
`ISystem` is minimal — `void Update(World world, double dt)` (`Engine/Core/ISystem.cs:5-8`); `IDrawSystem` adds a `Draw` (lines 14-17).

Doc-comment (lines 3-13): *"The engine does not enforce this at runtime. ## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-21 16:18 UTC

Agent 2's report is in — the Skia/packaging/profiling picture is clear:

- **`SkiaRenderer` is fully surface-agnostic** (takes an `SKSurface`, zero GL/SDL references, even `Distort` only uses `_surface.Snapshot`). So the WinForms backend can construct the *identical* renderer over an `SKGLControl` surface — GDI+ gets deleted, no renderer rewrite.
- **Native deps are minimal and Linux-only today:** SkiaSharp 2.88.7 (only the Linux native asset referenced), Silk.NET.SDL needs a *system* libSDL2, GL from system libs. No `SkiaSharp.NativeAssets.Win32/macOS`, no `SkiaSharp.Views.*`, **no RIDs / self-contained / publish settings, and no `.sln`** anywhere. That's the gap for cross-platform packaging.
- **No per-system profiling exists** — but the systems are a plain `ISystem[]` iterated in one `foreach` in `PlayingState`, so wrapping each `Update` in a `Stopwatch` is a clean drop-in. `ISystemMetadata`/`ComputationModel` exist but are unused; `ForceLog` is a good pattern to mirror for a timing sink; the demo already has an FPS-counter pattern.

Still waiting on the FX/zones/slow-mo/minimap explorer and the repo-layout/editor-coupling explorer before I finalize — those pin down the slow-mo bug, the minimap data path, and exactly which engine APIs are editor-only (the cleanup scope).

# GameEngine finalization-sprint exploration

## 1. Vortex warp + gusts

### `VortexFx.cs` — the whole effect
File: `/home/sebastian/ADVANCED/workspace/GameEngine/Gameplay/VortexFx.cs`

**Mote struct + storage** (`VortexFx.cs:16-23`): `Mote { Pos, Prev, Vel; Life, MaxLife }`, pooled `Mote[] _motes = new Mote[256]` with live `_count`, plus `_gustTimer`. `Reset()` zeroes count and timer.

**Spawn + advect + age** — `Update(...)` (`VortexFx.cs:26-68`):
- Early-outs when `!cfg.Enabled`; grows the pool to `cfg.MaxMotes` (`:29-30`).
- Advection loop (`:34-47`): `m.Life -= dt`; `m.Vel = field(m.Pos) * cfg.SpeedScale`; `m.Pos += m.Vel * dt`. A mote dies when life ≤ 0, when within `30f` of the eye (`(m.Pos-centre).LengthSquared() &lt; 30f*30f`), or when it leaves the view rect plus `margin = 300f` (`:33, :41-44`).
- Gust bursts (`:50-67`): `_gustTimer` counts down; reset to `MathF.Max(0.05f, cfg.GustInterval * jitter)` where `jitter = 1 ± cfg.GustJitter` (`:53-54`). Each burst spawns up to `cfg.MotesPerGust` (capped by `cfg.MaxMotes`). Spawn is **area-uniform in a disc**: `r = cfg.MaxRadius * sqrt(rand)`, random angle (`:59-61`) → denser further out. Off-screen spawns are skipped (`:62-63`). TTL = `MathF.Max(0.2f, cfg.Ttl * (1 ± cfg.TtlJitter))` (`:64`).

**DrawStreaks** (`VortexFx.cs:71-90`) — world space:
- Colour bytes from `cfg.Color[0..2]` with fallbacks 150/130/240 (`:74-76`).
- Per mote: `k = Life/MaxLife`; `fade = k*(1-k)*4` (ease in/out); alpha `= 200f * fade` (`:80-82`). Streak tail = `m.Pos - m.Vel * cfg.StreakSeconds`, drawn as a 2px line (`:84-85`).
- Eye core: `FillCircle(centre, 6f, ...210)` + `DrawCircle(centre, 13f, ...120, 2f)` (`:88-89`).

**DrawWarp** — the swirl (`VortexFx.cs:93-116`), screen space:
- Guards: `post != null &amp;&amp; cfg.Enabled &amp;&amp; cfg.WarpEnabled &amp;&amp; cfg.WarpStrength &gt; 0` (`:95`).
- **Region size**: `rad = cfg.WarpRadius * camera.Zoom`; the distort rect is `eye ± (rad,rad)` centred on `camera.WorldToScreen(centre)` (`:97-99`), with off-screen cull (`:101`). Grid resolution = `cfg.WarpGrid × cfg.WarpGrid` (`:105`).
- **Twist falloff**: `t = 1 - sqrt(d²)/rad` (1 at eye → 0 at edge); `ang = strength * t*t` — twist grows quadratically toward the centre (`:110-111`). Sampling rotates BACKWARD to appear rotated forward (`:113-114`).

### `VortexFxConfig` model
File: `/home/sebastian/ADVANCED/workspace/GameEngine/GameConfig/Models/WaveSystemConfig.cs:142-168`. Knobs (defaults):

| Knob | Field:line | Controls |
|---|---|---|
| mote count / burst | `MotesPerGust=14` (`:148`), `MaxMotes=400` (`:149`), `GustInterval=0.5` (`:146`), `GustJitter=0.6` (`:147`) | how many motes and how often |
| **mote spread** | `MaxRadius=1600` (`:154`) | spawn-disc radius around the eye |
| mote lifetime | `Ttl=2.2` (`:150`), `TtlJitter=0.4` (`:151`) | fade duration |
| streak length | `StreakSeconds=0.09` (`:156`) | tail length in seconds of velocity |
| speed | `SpeedScale=1` (`:158`) | visual advection multiplier |
| **opacity** | `Color=[150,130,240]` (`:159`) | RGB; alpha itself is hard-coded (`200f*fade` streaks, 210/120 core — not config-driven) |
| **warp radius** | `WarpRadius=620` (`:164`) | swirl disc radius (× zoom) |
| **warp strength** | `WarpStrength=0.6` (`:166`) | peak twist radians at eye |
| warp on/off + grid | `WarpEnabled=true` (`:162`), `WarpGrid=20` (`:167`) | distortion mesh density |

Note: there is **no** direct opacity knob — streak/core alphas are literals in `DrawStreaks`. Only colour is configurable.

### JSON — `vortexFx` block
File: `/home/sebastian/ADVANCED/workspace/GameEngine/Assets/game_config.json:1026-1046`. Live values match defaults exactly: `gustInterval 0.5, gustJitter 0.6, motesPerGust 14, maxMotes 400, ttl 2.2, ttlJitter 0.4, maxRadius 1600, streakSeconds 0.09, speedScale 1.0, color [150,130,240], warpEnabled true, warpRadius 620, warpStrength 0.6, warpGrid 20`.

Wiring: constructed as `_vortexFx = new()` (`PlayingState.cs:29`); ticked at `PlayingState.cs:328` (`_vortexFx.Update((float)gameDt, _vortex.Centre, _vortex.FieldVelocity, _ctx.Config.VortexFx, ...)`); drawn at `PlayingState.cs:408` (`DrawStreaks`) and `:411` (`DrawWarp`).

## 2. Slow-mo timer bug

File: `/home/sebastian/ADVANCED/workspace/GameEngine/GameCore/States/PlayingState.cs`, `Update(double dt)` (`:240-399`).

**How `gameDt` is derived** (`:274-280`):
```csharp
double gameDt = dt;
if (_world.IsAlive(_player) &amp;&amp; _world.HasComponent&lt;SkillState&gt;(_player))
{
    float slowActive = _world.GetComponent&lt;SkillState&gt;(_player).SlowMoActive;
    if (slowActive &gt; 0f &amp;&amp; _ctx.Config.Skills.TryGetValue("slowmo", out var smCfg))
        gameDt *= smCfg.TimeScale ?? 0.3;
}
```
`SlowMoActive` is a remaining-duration counter (`GameComponents.cs:69`). `TimeScale` = `0.3` from `SkillConfig.cs:20` / JSON `skills.slowmo.timeScale = 0.3` (`game_config.json:234`).

**Which dt each consumer gets:**

| Consumer | line | dt used |
|---|---|---|
| Systems loop (`s.Update(_world, gameDt)`) incl. `PlayerControlSystem`, `BorderHazardSystem`, physics, `_vortex` | `:282-291` | **scaled `gameDt`** |
| `_fx.Update((float)gameDt)` particles | `:293` | **scaled `gameDt`** |
| `_ctx.Score.Update(dt, ...)` | `:307` | real `dt` |
| Camera follow `k = 1-exp(-...*(float)dt)` | `:318` | real `dt` |
| `_camera.UpdateShake`, `UpdatePopups`, banner | `:321-323` | real `dt` |
| **Vortex FX** `_vortexFx.Update((float)gameDt, ...)` | `:328` | **scaled `gameDt`** |
| Game-over `_deadTimer += (float)dt` | `:334` | real `dt` |
| `_gameTime += (float)dt`; `_waveTimer += (float)dt` | `:346-347` | real `dt` |
| **`UpdateCampingResponse((float)dt)`** | `:350` | **real `dt`** |
| Wave countdown, special/mothership time gates | `:367, :377-389` | real `dt` |

**Camping/hunter timer accrual — confirmed:** `UpdateCampingResponse` is called with **real `dt`** (`:350`), and inside it `_campTime += dt` / `_campTime -= dt * cr.DecayRate` (`:746-747`). So the anti-camping/hunter timer accrues on **wall-clock time, NOT scaled `gameDt`** — slow-mo does not slow it.

**Slow-mo duration self-consumption (the subtle bug surface):** `SlowMoActive` is decremented inside `PlayerControlSystem` at `if (sk.SlowMoActive &gt; 0f) sk.SlowMoActive -= fdt` (`GameSystems.cs:67`), where `fdt = (float)dt` and that system is driven by **scaled `gameDt`** (`PlayingState.cs:290`). So the 3s slow-mo `duration` (`game_config.json:233`) counts down at 0.3× → it lasts ~10s of real time. Slow-mo is applied only to the systems loop, particles, and vortex FX; the wave director, `_gameTime`, camera, popups, and the camping timer all run on real `dt`. `SlowMoActive` is also read for movement/rotation boosts at `GameSystems.cs:132, :160`, set to `Duration` at `:100`.

## 3. Border danger zones

### `BorderHazardSystem` — erosion geometry
File: `/home/sebastian/ADVANCED/workspace/GameEngine/Gameplay/Systems/GameSystems.cs:348-490`.
- Pass 1 (`:375-420`): damp outward velocity (`DampZone`), inward push (`PushZone/PushStrength`), hard-clamp position to `[0,0]..(W,H)` (`:419`). Projectiles are destroyed at the edge (`:382-390`); `InboundSpawn` bodies are exempt until they cross in (`:395-399`).
- Pass 2 erosion (`:422-476`): `hz = _cfg.HazardZone` (`:423`). Per fracturable body, per-edge margins `mL=hz-x, mR=hz-(W-x), mT=hz-y, mB=hz-(H-y)`, `depth = max(all four)` (`:434-435`). If `depth&gt;0`, accrue `ex.Time`; past `Grace`, every `Tick` seconds fracture the most-exposed cell along the nearest outward normal with impactor `mass = BaseMass*(1+Ramp*extra)` (`:441-464`). Outside the rim, `ex.Time` decays by `DecayRate` (`:470`). Constructed at `PlayingState.cs:117`.

### `BorderHazardConfig`
`/home/sebastian/ADVANCED/workspace/GameEngine/GameConfig/Models/WaveSystemConfig.cs:186-231`; JSON `borderHazard` `game_config.json:992-1011`: `hazardZone 340, dampZone 200, pushZone 420, pushStrength 1200, grace 2.0, tick 0.4, tintMaxAlpha 120, warpEnabled true, warpStrength 7, warpFreq 0.03, warpSpeed 2.2, warpGrid 24`.

### `CampZoneDepth` + `_campInZone` — player depth into the camping band
`PlayingState.cs:765-783`:
```csharp
float band = _ctx.Config.BorderHazard.HazardZone + cr.ZoneDepth;   // :769
float dx = MathF.Min(p.X, wc.Width  - p.X);                        // :772
float dy = MathF.Min(p.Y, wc.Height - p.Y);                        // :773
float edge = Clamp((band - Min(dx,dy)) / band, 0, 1);              // :775
float cornerBand = band * Max(1f, cr.CornerScale);                 // :777
// corner term when both dx,dy &lt; cornerBand ...                    // :778-780
return MathF.Max(edge, corner);                                    // :782
```
`_campInZone = CampZoneDepth(p) &gt; 0f` (`:744`), consumed by `UpdateCampingResponse` (`:746`). Config `CampingResponseConfig` at `WaveSystemConfig.cs:40-64`; JSON `campingResponse` at `game_config.json:1069-1096` (`zoneDepth 300, cornerScale 1.8, triggerSeconds 3, tintMaxAlpha 70`).

### Full-screen tints
- **Grey camping tint** `DrawCampCue` (`PlayingState.cs:495-516`): early-outs unless `_campTime&gt;0.05 || _campInZone` (`:499`); `progress = _campTime/TriggerSeconds`; `intensity = max(_campInZone?0.30:0, progress)` (`:501-502`); `FillRect(0,0,ScreenW,ScreenH, Color(165,170,180, TintMaxAlpha*intensity))` (`:504-506`). Also draws top-centre `"EXPOSED — HUNTERS {secs}s"` (`:508-515`). Called at `:413`.
- **Red erosion tint** `DrawBorderCue` (`PlayingState.cs:429-488`), called at `:412`. Primary red tint (`:479-487`): player distance to nearest edge `dist = min(p.X, W-p.X, p.Y, H-p.Y)`; `depth = (zone-dist)/zone`; `FillRect(..., Color(190,30,30, hz.TintMaxAlpha*depth))`.

### World edges as lines — NOT drawn today
No edge-line rendering exists. The **only** edge visual is the secondary **heat-haze warp** on on-screen edges via `post.Distort` (`DrawBorderCue`, `PlayingState.cs:436-476`): `VEdge(0,+1)`, `VEdge(Width,-1)`, `HEdge(0,+1)`, `HEdge(Height,-1)` (`:474-475`), each a strip displaced by `amp*depth*sin(...)` (`:451-457`, `:466-472`). This is gated on `hz.WarpEnabled &amp;&amp; hz.WarpStrength &gt; 0.5`. `FillRect` helper is at `PlayingState.cs:1348-1350`. So edges are only ever a shimmer, never a line.

## 4. Minimap prerequisites

### Enumerate asteroids + area + colour
- **Tag/variant**: `AsteroidTag` and `AsteroidVariant { string Key }` (`/home/sebastian/ADVANCED/workspace/GameEngine/Gameplay/Components/Tags.cs:4, :11`). Added at spawn: `world.AddComponent(e, new AsteroidTag())` and `new AsteroidVariant { Key = typeKey }` (`AsteroidPrefab.cs:58-59`).
- **Enumerate** with `_world.ForEach&lt;AsteroidTag, FracturableBody&gt;(...)` — exact pattern already used in `CountLiveCells` (`PlayingState.cs:1020-1026`). Position via `Transform` component.
- **(a) Area**: `VoronoiTessellator.TotalArea(in FracturableBody body)` sums `c.Area` over cells (`/home/sebastian/ADVANCED/workspace/GameEngine/Engine/Destruction/VoronoiTessellator.cs:356-361`). (`TotalMass`/`ComputeInertia` also there, `:365-370`, `:374+`.)
- **(b) Material/colour**: material key from `AsteroidVariant.Key` → `_ctx.Config.Asteroids[key].Material` (`AsteroidConfig.Material`, default `"rock"`, `/home/sebastian/ADVANCED/workspace/GameEngine/GameConfig/Models/AsteroidConfig.cs:11`). Colour from `AsteroidPrefab.MaterialColor(string materialKey)` returning `BodyColor { Fill, Outline }` (`AsteroidPrefab.cs:75-82`): ice `(70,115,155)`, metal `(50,60,75)`, glass `(60,120,95)`, sand `(150,118,58)`, default rock `(64,58,52)`. The body already carries its `BodyColor` (set via `MaterialColor(ac.Material)` at `AsteroidPrefab.cs:57`), so you can also read it off the entity's colour component instead of re-deriving.

### World bounds
`WorldConfig.Width=5760, Height=3240, SpawnMargin=500` (`WaveSystemConfig.cs:173-179`; JSON `game_config.json:986-991`, note `cameraFollowSpeed` JSON=10 vs model default 4). Accessed as `_ctx.Config.World` throughout (e.g. `PlayingState.cs:91, :312-317`).

### HUD screen-space drawing
- `DrawHud(IRenderer r)` (`PlayingState.cs:1097-1135`) draws entirely in screen space (no camera transform pushed — the world transform is popped at `:409` before HUD). Called at `:417`.
- `FillRect(r, x, y, w, h, Color)` helper (`PlayingState.cs:1348-1350`) — builds a quad via `r.FillPolygon(stackalloc Vector2[]{...})`. This is the primitive for minimap panels/blips.
- `Camera.WorldToScreen(Vector2)` (`/home/sebastian/ADVANCED/workspace/GameEngine/Engine/Rendering/Camera.cs:71-73`): `(world - Position)*Zoom + Screen/2`. `ScreenToWorld` (`:66-68`), `Zoom` (`:18`), `ScreenWidth/Height` (`:20-21`), `GetViewMatrix` (`:60-63`). For a minimap you'd map world→minimap-rect directly (world/`WorldConfig`), not via the camera.

### Tags for plotting other actors
- **Player**: `PlayerTag` (`Tags.cs:3`); entity handle available as `_player` / `_fracture.Player` (`PlayingState.cs:37`), position via `Transform`.
- **Aliens**: `AlienTag` (`Tags.cs:5`) and `AlienVariant { Key }` (`Tags.cs:14`).
- **Boss/mothership**: `MothershpId { int Id; int InitialCockpitCount }` tags every mothership fragment (`GameComponents.cs:100`); `BossBrain { ShockwaveCd, ... }` marks each cockpit-bearing controller fragment (`GameComponents.cs:115-121`). The win check already enumerates bosses with `_world.ForEach&lt;BossBrain&gt;(...)` (`PlayingState.cs:394`). Group id tracked as `_mothershpGroupId` (`PlayingState.cs:991`).

All of `ForEach&lt;T&gt;`, `Transform`, `FillPolygon`, and `MeasureText`/`DrawText` are already used in this file, so a minimap can be built with existing primitives.

## 4. Coupling that would block a move (reference graph)

Directed dependency graph (A → B = A references B):

```
Game (SDL exe)      → GameCore, Platform/Sdl
Game.WinForms (exe) → GameCore, Platform/WinForms
GameCore            → AsteroidsEngine, GameConfig, Gameplay
Gameplay            → AsteroidsEngine, GameConfig
Platform/Sdl        → AsteroidsEngine
Platform/WinForms   → AsteroidsEngine
GameConfig          → (nothing)
AsteroidsEngine     → (nothing)
Demos/AsteroidDemo  → AsteroidsEngine, Platform/Sdl, GameConfig, Gameplay
```

Findings on what can move cleanly:
- **Neither Gameplay nor GameCore nor Game/Game.WinForms references anything demo-only.** Explicit grep across `Gameplay/`, `GameCore/`, `Game/`, `Game.WinForms/` for `Engine.Ui`, `Engine.State`, `GameLoop`, `AsteroidDemo` returned **nothing**. No back-coupling from game code into the editor-only areas.
- **The demo does NOT reference GameCore or Game.** It references only `AsteroidsEngine`, `Platform/Sdl`, `GameConfig`, `Gameplay`. So the two executables (`Game`, `Game.WinForms`) and the editor (`Demos/AsteroidDemo`) share only the lower libraries (Engine/Gameplay/GameConfig), not each other. Moving the exes and the demo out of `GameEngine/` requires only path fixups in their `ProjectReference` relative paths (`../../AsteroidsEngine.csproj`, etc.), since there is no `.sln` to update.
- **The editor-only engine APIs (GameLoop, Engine/State, Engine/Ui, Engine/Resources IResourceManager+AssetIds, and the InputSystem suppress/text/raw-mouse members) have no game-side consumers.** The only cross-cutting coupling is that the `Ui`/editor members live inside `AsteroidsEngine` (via `Engine/Ui/*` and members on `InputSystem`), and `InputSystem.SuppressKeyboard` is referenced by InputSystem's own key accessors — so extracting `SuppressKeyboard` specifically would touch `InputSystem.cs` internals, whereas `Engine/Ui/*`, `Engine/State/*`, `Engine/Core/GameLoop.cs`, and `Engine/Resources/{IResourceManager,AssetIds}.cs` are self-contained and referenced only by the demo (or nobody).

2. Key Technical Concepts:
   - ECS engine: World, structs in SparseSet, ref-based mutation, deferred destroy, `ISystem.Update(World, double dt)`, EventBus (deferred publish, flushed by EventFlushSystem).
   - PAL (Platform Abstraction Layer): `IGameWindow`, `IRenderer`, optional `IPostEffects` (feature-detected via `renderer as IPostEffects`), `IAudioBackend` (stub).
   - Two backends: SDL2+SkiaSharp (GPU/GL, cross-platform) and WinForms+GDI+ (net8.0-windows). `SkiaRenderer` is surface-agnostic (takes an `SKSurface`).
   - `GameHost.Run(IGameWindow)` = shared bootstrap + fixed-timestep loop (1/120s); `FixedTimestep.Advance` can return 0..N steps/frame.
   - Fixed timestep + slow-mo: systems run on scaled `gameDt`; some timers on real `dt`.
   - Vortex FX (`VortexFx.cs`): gust motes advected along `VortexSystem.FieldVelocity`, drawn as fading streaks; screen-space swirl warp via `IPostEffects.Distort`.
   - Border hazard zones: erosion (`hazardZone`), camping/hunter band (`hazardZone + zoneDepth`), corner widening (`cornerScale`).
   - Restructured repo layout: `src/` (libs), `apps/` (exes), `tools/Editor`, `Assets/`, `AsteroidsOnSteroids.sln`. Build commands: `dotnet build AsteroidsOnSteroids.sln`; `cd apps/Game.Sdl && dotnet run`; `cd tools/Editor && dotnet run`.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-22 09:02 UTC

The system does one `PhysicsQueries.Raycast` per bullet. The cost blows up with bullet count — grenade shrapnel are bullets. Let me see what a single raycast costs:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-22 09:03 UTC

This is the smoking gun. `PhysicsQueries.Raycast` at [PhysicsQueries.cs:54](src/Engine/Systems/PhysicsQueries.cs#L54) is a **brute-force scan of every collider in the world**, and for each asteroid (a `CompoundShape`) it walks all that body's cells. So `RaycastBulletSystem`'s cost is:

```
bullets  ×  all colliders in world  ×  cells-per-asteroid
```

A grenade spawns ~260 shrapnel — and **shrapnel carry `BulletTag`, so each is a full raycast**. With 479 live cells that product explodes → 237 ms. It's O(N×M), exactly the shape of your numbers (15 ms at 149 cells → 237 ms at 479 cells + more bullets). Let me check whether `CompoundShape.Raycast` already AABB-gates, since that decides the cheapest fix:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-22 09:04 UTC

Confirmed the mechanism from the code. There are **two** missing culling layers stacked on top of each other, which is why it's so violent:

## Root cause: `RaycastBulletSystem` is O(bullets × colliders × cells)

1. **No broad phase per entity.** [PhysicsQueries.Raycast:54](src/Engine/Systems/PhysicsQueries.cs#L54) does `world.ForEach<Transform, Collider>` — every bullet tests *every collider in the world*, even ones nowhere near its ray.
2. **No AABB gate per cell.** [CompoundShape.Raycast:262](src/Engine/Collision/CompoundShape.cs#L262) then loops **every cell** of each asteroid and does a full polygon raycast — no bounding-box reject. Notably, its sibling `SegmentParts` at [line 213](src/Engine/Collision/CompoundShape.cs#L213) *already* does exactly the AABB gate this method is missing.

Grenade shrapnel carry `BulletTag`, so ~260 of them each pay that full product every frame until TTL. That's your 15 ms → 237 ms curve as cell count climbs.

## What I'd do — two cheap, feel-neutral fixes (no architecture change)

**Fix A — whole-shape AABB gate in `PhysicsQueries.Raycast`.** Before calling `c.Shape.Raycast`, do an O(1) segment-vs-AABB test against `c.Shape.GetAABB(...)` (already exists, line 170). Most colliders aren't on the ray line → rejected in a handful of ops. Turns the outer loop from `colliders × cells` into `colliders (cheap) + few-hits × cells`.

**Fix B — per-part AABB gate inside `CompoundShape.Raycast`.** Copy the pattern from `SegmentParts`: compute the ray's local-space segment AABB, `continue` on any cell whose `_localAabbs[i]` doesn't overlap it. So even when a ray *does* cross an asteroid, it only tests the few cells along its path, not all ~50.

Together these make it effectively linear in bullets and near-constant per raycast. I expect the 237 ms case to drop to low single-digit ms. **This is the whole fix I'd ship first** — it doesn't touch feel, doesn't change results (same nearest-hit), just skips work that can't contribute.

**Fix C (hold unless still needed):** route the raycast through the existing `SpatialGrid` (DDA traversal of grid cells the ray crosses) so we don't even iterate all colliders. Proper, but more code and it interacts with when the grid is rebuilt (RaycastBulletSystem runs before CollisionSystem, so it'd read a 1-frame-stale grid — fine for bullets, but worth confirming). A+B alone likely make C unnecessary.

## VortexWarp — flat ~3.6 ms whenever the eye is on-screen

It's load-independent (3.63 ms at 218 cells, 3.01 ms at 479) — a GPU snapshot + mesh every frame. Two honest choices:
- **Drop the warp, keep the gust streaks** (the streaks are cheap and already sell the vortex read). Reclaims a flat 3.6 ms. Given you're leaning this way, I think this is the right call — the warp is a subtle effect paying a fixed frame tax.
- Or shrink it (smaller snapshot region + coarser `WarpGrid`) if you want to keep some swirl. I'd only do this if you decide the effect matters.

**CollisionSystem** (0.44 ms peak) — agreed, leave it.

My recommendation: **A + B now** (kills the real bottleneck), and **remove the warp** (keep streaks). Want me to go ahead with those, or drop the warp entirely including the streaks?

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-22 09:24 UTC

Good — I dug into the grid and the surrounding systems. Here's the lay of the land and my honest read on both ideas.

## What the grid actually is today

- `SpatialGrid` is a **uniform** grid, `cellSize = 160`, constructed inline and owned **privately by `CollisionSystem`** ([PlayingState.cs:129](src/GameCore/States/PlayingState.cs#L129)), rebuilt from scratch (`Clear()` + `Insert`) at the top of `CollisionSystem.Update` every frame.
- Its only query is `GetCandidates(min, max)` — an **AABB region query**. There is no ray/segment traversal.
- **Ordering matters:** `RaycastBulletSystem` runs at [line 124](src/GameCore/States/PlayingState.cs#L124), `CollisionSystem` at [line 129](src/GameCore/States/PlayingState.cs#L129) — bullets run *before* the grid is built.

## Q1 — share the grid for the raycast broad phase

Worth doing, but the load-bearing issue isn't the query, it's **who builds the index and when**:

- **Staleness is a correctness trap, not just perf.** If the raycast reads the grid `CollisionSystem` built, it's reading **last frame's** grid (raycast runs first). Fragments spawn constantly during fracture (sand re-ghosts every few frames — the whole grace/ghost mask exists so bullets *see* fresh fragments). A stale grid means bullets **tunnel through brand-new fragments** for a frame or two. That's a gameplay regression, not a rounding error.
- **So the real change is a refactor:** hoist the broad-phase build into a single *first-in-frame* step (its own tiny `SpatialIndexSystem`, or a shared index PlayingState owns), have **both** the raycast and `CollisionSystem` consume it, and stop `CollisionSystem` from rebuilding. Then both read a fresh, this-frame index. Moderate but well-contained.
- **The query itself is easy and needs no DDA.** Bullets travel a bounded distance per frame (v · 1/120s), so a bullet's segment AABB is ~1 cell. Just `GetCandidates(segmentMin, segmentMax)` then run the existing exact raycast on those few candidates. A proper ray-traversal API would only matter for long rays, which we don't have. (One caveat: `GetCandidates` dedups with `results.Contains` — O(k²) — fine for a handful of candidates, would hurt only on big queries.)

**But:** the gain here is *smaller* than A+B was. A+B already turned the per-bullet cost from `colliders × cells` into `colliders × cheap-AABB`. The grid removes the "× all colliders" scan too — real during a 260-shrapnel grenade, but possibly a couple of ms, and the grenade's cost may now be dominated by the 260 `BeginFracture` calls + particle spawns instead. **I'd re-profile the post-A+B `RaycastBulletSystem` first** (fresh `Z`/`X` dump on a grenade) and only do the grid refactor if it's still the top line. Measure, don't assume — same discipline that just paid off.

## Q2 — adaptive grid, now?

I'd **hold off.** Reasoning:

- The grid exists to accelerate **`CollisionSystem`**, which the profile shows at **0.44 ms even at 479 cells** — it is *not* a bottleneck. Adaptive sizing optimizes the thing that isn't hurting.
- The **raycast use creates no pressure for adaptivity** — short segments touch 1–2 cells at almost any sane cell size.
- The pain an adaptive grid would address is the game's **bimodal** size range (huge rocks + tiny sand at once). A *simple* per-frame global cell-size (recompute from the entity distribution) is cheap and low-risk but **won't help the bimodal case** — one global size still can't suit both. The version that *would* (hierarchical / loose grid, the "Quadtree (future)" the interface hints at) is a large, bug-prone change to a **currently-correct** collision path, where bugs = missed pairs = tunnelling.

So: real adaptivity is a lot of risk against **zero measured collision cost**. Classic YAGNI. I'd defer it until a profile shows the broad phase itself is the cost — and revisit it as "quadtree/loose grid" rather than "resize the uniform grid," which is the change that would actually pay.

## Suggested next moves

1. **Remove the VortexWarp** (agreed — reclaims the flat 3.6 ms; keep the cheap gust streaks).
2. **Re-profile** the post-A+B grenade case (`Z` then `X`) so we see what `RaycastBulletSystem` actually costs now.
3. **Then decide the grid share** on the number: still the top line → do the first-in-frame shared-index refactor; already sub-2 ms → bank A+B and move to Phase 3.
4. **Adaptive grid → deferred follow-up**, revisited as a quadtree only if the broad phase ever profiles hot.

Want me to pull the warp now and leave the grid share pending your fresh grenade dump?

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-22 09:52 UTC

I'm on board — this is the right call, and it's a genuinely durable design rather than a band-aid. My view on the shape, then I'll build it:

**The design (one broad phase, built once, shared):**
- A new engine **`BroadPhaseSystem`** rebuilds one shared `ISpatialIndex` each frame, inserted **right after movement, before the raycast** — so both consumers read a fresh, post-movement snapshot.
- **`CollisionSystem` stops building its own** index and just queries the shared one.
- **`ISpatialIndex` gets a `QuerySegment(from, to)`** (DDA cell-walk) so a ray only touches the cells it crosses — and O(1) dedup instead of the current O(k²) `Contains`, so it stays cheap as N grows.
- **The raycast queries the index** instead of scanning all colliders, keeping the A+B AABB gates as the narrow filter.
- **Key scaling property:** bullets/shrapnel have no `Collider`, so they're never *in* the index — more shrapnel just means more O(1) queries. The whole thing becomes linear in entities **and** linear in bullets, independent of each other. That's your "never optimize again."

The only subtlety is **ordering** (build after movement, before both consumers) — I worked out the exact slot. Let me implement across engine + both apps.

- **`CollisionSystem`** no longer builds its own index — it queries the shared one.
- Wired in **both** `PlayingState` and the `Editor`.

Raycast cost is now linear in bullets and linear in entities, decoupled. Cranking `shrapnelCount` should no longer move `RaycastBulletSystem` the way it did.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-22 10:11 UTC

Warp gone — kept the streaks + eye core. Now your question, which is a good one:

## Why the systems don't sum to the frame time

The profiler is a **relative hotspot finder, not a 100%-accounting budget.** The frame stopwatch (`_frameSw`, committed at Draw start) measures the *entire* wall-clock loop iteration, but only the fixed-step **systems** and a couple of **draw phases** are instrumented. Everything else in [GameHost.Run](src/GameCore/GameHost.cs#L47)'s loop is invisible to it. In rough order of size:

1. **`window.Present()` — untimed, and it's where the GPU actually works.** Skia only *records* draw commands on the CPU during `Draw.World` (that's why it's a mere ~0.5 ms — it's building a command list, not drawing). The GPU rasterizes the whole scene at `Canvas.Flush()` / `GLSwapWindow()` inside `Present()`. So the true render cost is **split**: recording → `Draw.World` (timed), execution → `Present` (untimed). The warp made this worse — its `Snapshot()` forced a mid-frame GPU sync — which is why removing it should help more than the 4 ms the timer showed.

2. **Untimed draw calls.** Only `Draw.World` (and formerly the warp) were wrapped. `DrawStreaks` (up to 900 mote segments), `DrawHud`, `DrawMinimap` (re-iterates asteroids), `DrawZoneEdges`, banner/popups, `Begin/End` — none are timed.

3. **The soft frame cap `Thread.Sleep`** ([GameHost.cs:76](src/GameCore/GameHost.cs#L76)). When a frame finishes under 8.33 ms it sleeps to the next 120 Hz slot — idle time counted in the frame clock but owned by no system. This dominates the gap when you're running fast/capped, and is zero during a grenade. (In your 70 fps calm capture the frame was 14 ms > 8.33, so it *didn't* sleep — there the gap is Present + untimed draws.)

4. **`PollEvents()` + `input.BeginFrame()`** — the OS event pump, untimed.

5. **Fixed-timestep substep variance.** `fixedStep.Advance()` returns **0..N** steps; the profiler *sums* system times across whatever ran that frame. A render frame with 0 sim steps shows ~0 system time but a full frame clock; one with 2 steps double-counts. So summed-systems ÷ frame-time floats with the sim/render ratio — they aren't the same clock.

6. GC/JIT/OS jitter lands in wall-clock only.

**If you want the gap made visible,** I can add two cheap things to the overlay: time `window.Present()` (the likely biggest hidden line now that the warp's gone) and an **`Unaccounted = frame − Σsections`** row. Want that? It'd turn the profiler into a near-complete budget.

Otherwise — do a fresh `Z`/`X` with `shrapnelCount` at 1260 to confirm the grid holds `RaycastBulletSystem` flat, and we move to **Phase 3** (WinForms→Skia + packaging).

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-22 12:40 UTC

Confirmed the theory in the code. Here's what the new instrumentation reveals.

## The grid was a total success

At **`shrapnelCount 1260`** (~4000 particles), `RaycastBulletSystem` is now **1.4–1.9 ms** — versus **237 ms** in the old brute-force world. It barely moves with bullet count. The broad phase decoupled raycast cost from entity count exactly as intended. That axis is done.

## The new bottleneck is rendering — specifically draw-call count

Look at the heavy captures: no single *system* is hot. The frame is dominated by **`unaccounted`** (7 ms baseline → **21 ms** during a grenade), and it tracks **`worldDraws`** almost linearly:

| worldDraws | particles | unaccounted | frame |
|---|---|---|---|
| 694 | 52 | 7.7 ms | 13.5 ms |
| 5602 | 3947 | 15.4 ms | 24.5 ms |
| 8349 | 4095 | 21.6 ms | 30.4 ms |

≈ **1.8 µs of GPU/driver overhead per draw call** above a fixed baseline.

**Root cause, confirmed in code:** [`ParticleSystem.Draw`](src/Engine/Effects/ParticleSystem.cs#L61) issues **one `FillCircle` per particle**, and bullet tracers are **3 draws each** ([WorldRenderer.cs:190](src/Gameplay/WorldRenderer.cs#L190)). So 4000 particles + ~1000 shrapnel tracers = **~8000 individual GPU draws**. Recording them on the CPU is cheap (`Draw.World` is only 2.5 ms — Skia just builds a command list), but the GPU *executes* thousands of tiny draws at the `r.End()` flush, which is untimed → it lands in `unaccounted` (plus a bit in `Present`). The ~7 ms baseline is the same thing for the vortex streaks (up to 900 untimed `DrawLine`s every frame).

So under heavy fracture the game is now **draw-call bound**, and particles are the overwhelming majority of the draws.

## Where I'd go — and a choice for you

The "never optimize again" fix that matches your grid philosophy is **batched particle rendering**: collapse thousands of per-particle `FillCircle` calls into **one** GPU submission. In Skia that's `DrawVertices`/`DrawAtlas` with per-particle color — I'd expose it as an optional `IParticleBatch` capability (feature-detected like `IPostEffects`, with the current per-circle loop as the CPU fallback). Expected effect: the ~15–20 ms particle cost during a grenade collapses to a fraction of a ms, and it stays flat no matter how many particles. Same treatment can batch the vortex streaks.

The one tradeoff: batched particles become textured quads (an atlas sprite) rather than true `FillCircle`s. For 2–4 px motes that's visually indistinguishable, but it *is* a rendering-path change to the destruction VFX, so I want your call.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-22 12:54 UTC

That's a decisive win. Look at what batching did:

| | before | after |
|---|---|---|
| particles | 4095 | 3207 |
| **world draw calls** | **8349** | **1435** |
| frame | 30.4 ms | **13.4 ms** |
| FPS | 33 | **75** |

And the crucial part — **`worldDraws` is now essentially independent of particle count**: 256 particles → 1408 draws, 3207 particles → 1435 draws. The entire particle load collapsed to ~1 draw call. A 1260-shrapnel grenade now runs at 56–75 FPS instead of 33. That axis is done and it *scales* — exactly the goal.

## What's left is steady rendering cost, not a scaling bottleneck

The two top lines now are **`Present` (3–6 ms)** and **`unaccounted` (7–8 ms)**, and neither explodes with entity/particle count anymore:

- **`Present`** tracks on-screen **cell geometry** (6.4 ms at 696 cells vs 3.3 ms at 313) — it's genuine GPU rasterization/overdraw of the asteroid meshes. Bounded by what's visible, not by the fracture storm.
- **`unaccounted`** is now the biggest black box: the untimed **Draw tail** — vortex streaks (up to ~900 `DrawLine`s every frame), the minimap (a dot per asteroid), the HUD, plus the `r.End()` GPU flush and `PollEvents`. It's a roughly *fixed* cost, not a scaling one.

Since `unaccounted` is now the #1 line and entirely uninstrumented, I don't want to guess at it. The disciplined move is **one cheap instrumentation pass**: wrap the post-`Draw.World` draws as `Draw.Overlay` and time `r.End()` as `Draw.Flush`. That splits `unaccounted` into "untimed CPU draws (streaks/minimap/HUD)" vs "GPU flush" vs "loop overhead" — then we know whether batching the streaks/tracers is worth it or whether it's just GPU fill we can't cheaply cut.

The remaining levers, once we can see them:
- **Bullet tracers** — the other ~half of `worldDraws`, constant-color lines → batchable to ~2 `DrawPoints` calls.
- **Vortex streaks** — ~900 untimed lines; cap the mote count or batch them.

But honestly: **the game is now shipping-smooth** (56–75 FPS in your heaviest stress case with 5× normal shrapnel). This is a fine place to stop optimizing and start Phase 3.

Which do you want — **(a)** add the `Draw.Overlay`/`Draw.Flush` timers so we can see inside `unaccounted` and decide on streak/tracer batching, or **(b)** call Phase 2 done and move to **Phase 3** (WinForms→Skia + cross-platform packaging)?

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-22 13:03 UTC

- **`Draw.Overlay`** — all the post-world screen draws (vortex streaks, rim cues, HUD, minimap, banners/popups). The debug overlay itself is excluded so it doesn't pollute the number.
- **`Draw.Flush`** — `r.End()`, where Skia flushes the command buffer and the GPU rasterizes the frame. Separated so we can tell **CPU command-recording** cost (the `Draw.*` sections) from **GPU execution** cost.

This carves `unaccounted` into three readable pieces:
- `Draw.Overlay` → untimed CPU draws (if this is big, batching streaks/minimap pays off),
- `Draw.Flush` → GPU rasterization/overdraw (if this dominates, it's fill-bound — batching won't help, only less geometry/overdraw would),
- whatever's *still* unaccounted → `PollEvents`, `input.BeginFrame`, and the soft-cap `Thread.Sleep`.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-22 13:07 UTC
