## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-22 13:08 UTC

The split is revealing — and the answer is a bit counterintuitive. Two things:

**`unaccounted` collapsed to ~1 ms** — so the entire gap really was the draw tail. Good.

**But `Draw.Overlay` (21 ms / 7.45 ms) is not what it looks like.** The overlay draws nothing particle-related — streaks, HUD, minimap, cues are the same regardless of the fracture storm. Yet `Draw.Overlay` **tracks particle count**: 21 ms at 4003 particles vs 7.45 ms at 891. That can only mean one thing: it's a **GPU flush-stall**. Skia records `Draw.World`'s commands on the CPU cheaply (2.3 ms), but the first draw *after* World forces the GPU to catch up — so the GPU cost of World's work surfaces inside `Draw.Overlay`. In other words:

> **`Draw.Overlay` + `Draw.Flush` + `Present` ≈ 29 ms is the *true GPU frame*, and it's fill-rate bound.**

Comparing the two captures isolates the culprit: **+3100 particles → +~14 ms of GPU fill.** Batching removed the *draw-call* overhead (8349 → 1), but 4003 large soft-alpha sprites still **overdraw** an enormous number of blended pixels — one draw call, but massive fill. Cells add a baseline (the union-underlay + per-cell + inflation in [DrawBodies](src/Gameplay/WorldRenderer.cs#L109) draws each cell region ~2× on purpose, to hide seams).

**This is fill rate, not call overhead — batching can't touch it.** The only levers are *fewer / smaller / shorter-lived* particles (less blended area), or less cell overdraw (a visual-quality tradeoff).

## The honest bottom line

At **ship settings** (`shrapnelCount 260`, ~800 particles) the game is smooth — your 891-particle capture is 52 FPS *with 1128 cells on screen*. The **1260 shrapnel + 1047 cells** case is a 5× stress test, and its residual cost is raw GPU pixel fill, which is inherent, not architectural.

One clean, philosophy-matching lever remains if you want the stress case smoother: **cap the live-particle budget.** `ParticleSystem` currently hard-drops at capacity 4096 ([ParticleSystem.cs:39](src/Engine/Effects/ParticleSystem.cs#L39)); lowering that (or shrinking grenade dust size) *bounds particle fill regardless of grenade size*. Combined with batching, particle cost becomes O(1) in **both** draw calls and fill — truly "never optimize again." At normal settings you'd never hit the cap, so no visual change in real play.

Your call:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-22 13:37 UTC

Great questions — let me answer all three from the actual code. First, how cells are drawn and the full particle inventory:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-22 13:39 UTC

Excellent instinct — and the data backs it up. Let me answer all three precisely.

## 1. How a 30-cell asteroid is filled — yes, ~31 GPU calls

Per visible body, [DrawBodies](src/Gameplay/WorldRenderer.cs#L109) issues:
- **1 `FillPath`** — the union underlay (all cells as one path), to hide seams.
- **N `FillPolygon`** — one per cell, each *inflated* from its centroid so neighbours overlap.
- plus crack lines for broken bonds.

So a 30-cell asteroid = **~31 draw calls + ~2× area overdraw** (union fill, then every cell filled again on top, inflated). 

**This is now the dominant draw-call cost.** In your `ast 1128c` capture, `worldDraws` is 1281 — and ~1150 of those are cells (union + per-cell). Particles collapsed to 1. **Cells are now ~90% of all draw calls**, and they self-overdraw 2×.

## 2. Your triangulation idea is exactly right — and it's the highest-value move now

Decompose each body into a **cached triangle mesh, built once per split**:
- Cells are **convex**, so each triangulates trivially by a fan (`n−2` triangles, no ear-clipping).
- Build one per-body buffer — vertices in body-local space + a per-vertex color (the cell's `FillColor`) — and cache it on the entity; rebuild **only** when the body fractures/splits.
- Draw the whole asteroid in **one `DrawVertices` call** (the canvas matrix applies the body transform), which `SkiaRenderer` already does for the warp.

Effect: **~31 calls → 1 per body** (1150 → ~35 total), **and the 2× overdraw disappears** — a shared-edge triangle mesh has no seams, so the union underlay and the cell inflation both become unnecessary. This directly attacks both the draw-call count *and* the cell fill. It's the cell-side equivalent of what batching did for particles, and it matches your "do it once per split" instinct exactly.

## 3. The particle inventory + fill cost

Fill per sprite ≈ `(2·radius)²` px (the soft atlas fills its whole quad with alpha blend). Sources:

| Source | Emitted | Radius (px) | TTL | Fill each | Verdict |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| **Bullet trail** ([GameSystems.cs:633](src/Gameplay/Systems/GameSystems.cs#L633)) | **1 per bullet, every frame** | 1.6→0.2 | 0.07–0.12 s | ~10 px² | Dominant **count** in a grenade (~1260/frame), trivial fill |
| **Spark** ([EmitSparks](src/Gameplay/ParticleEffects.cs#L42)) | 9×e (≤~20) per impact | 2.0 | 0.2 s | ~16 px² | Cheap |
| **Dust** ([EmitDustBurst](src/Gameplay/ParticleEffects.cs#L68)) | 2–14 per pulverized/debris cell | 2.6 | **0.7 s** | ~27 px² | Long TTL → **accumulates to thousands**; moderate fill |
| **Flash** ([EmitFlash](src/Gameplay/ParticleEffects.cs#L27)) | 1 per impact / collision / detonation | **22–55** | 0.12 s | **1,900–12,000 px²** | **The fill king** |

**A single flash fills ~100–1000× a dust mote.** During a 1260-shrapnel grenade, hundreds of impacts land within ~0.1 s → **hundreds of 22–55 px flashes overlapping at once** = millions of blended pixels/frame. That, not the tiny trails, is the particle fill spike. So:
- **Count** is dominated by bullet trails (cheap fill).
- **Fill** is dominated by **flashes** (huge), then accumulated **dust** (long TTL).

The right particle lever isn't a blunt global cap — it's **shrink/cap the flashes** (and maybe trim dust TTL), which barely changes the look but removes most of the overdraw.

## Recommended plan

Both are worth doing, and each is a clean, durable engine change:

1. **Cell mesh batching** (your idea) — cached per-body triangle mesh, rebuilt on split, drawn in 1 `DrawVertices`. Kills ~90% of draw calls **and** the 2× cell overdraw. Biggest structural win; also speeds dense-asteroid scenes that *aren't* grenades.
2. **Flash fill tuning** — cap concurrent/size of flashes (and dust TTL). Cheap, kills most of the grenade fill spike.

I'd do **#1 first** (it's the meatier, higher-value change and addresses the now-dominant draw-call cost), then #2. Want me to start on the cell mesh batching — a cached `BodyMesh` component built at spawn/split + a `DrawVertices` path in `WorldRenderer`/`SkiaRenderer`?

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-22 14:00 UTC

Cell mesh batching is in and building green. What it does:

- **`IMeshBatch`** ([src/Engine/Rendering/IMeshBatch.cs](src/Engine/Rendering/IMeshBatch.cs)) — new optional capability; `SkiaRenderer` implements it via a single `DrawVertices`.
- **`WorldRenderer.DrawBodies` is now two passes** ([WorldRenderer.cs:88](src/Gameplay/WorldRenderer.cs#L88)):
  1. **`DrawBodyFills`** — fan-triangulates *every visible body's* live cells into **one shared world-space mesh** and submits it in **one** `FillMesh` call. Replaces the old per-body union-`FillPath` + N inflated `FillPolygon`s.
  2. **`DrawBodyOutlines`** — silhouettes + cracks on top (unchanged).
- Cells keep the tiny `CellPad` inflation, so neighbours still overlap → **no seams** even without the union underlay, which lets me drop it entirely → **true 1× fill** instead of 2×.
- AA is off on the mesh (adjacent triangles tile exactly); the AA outline pass draws the clean silhouette.
- Full CPU fallback (union + per-cell) kept for any backend without `IMeshBatch`.

**Expected effect:** `world draw calls` for cells drop from ~1150 to **1**, and cell GPU **fill halves** (no more union underlay). Combined with particle batching, `Draw.World`'s draw-call output should now be a tiny constant, and the cell contribution to `Draw.Overlay`/`Draw.Flush` (the GPU flush) should roughly halve.

Two things to check on a fresh `Z`/`X`:
1. **`world draw calls`** should crater (cells → 1, particles → 1; only outlines/tracers remain).
2. **Visual integrity** — look hard at asteroids for any hairline seams between cells or a jagged silhouette. The `CellPad` inflation + outline pass should prevent both, but this is the thing to verify since I changed the fill path.

Paste the capture (heavy grenade + dense field). If it looks clean and fast, the remaining lever is the flash-fill tuning (B2f) — or we call Phase 2 done and start Phase 3.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-22 14:06 UTC

Now the picture is complete and clean. `unaccounted` is 0.5 ms — everything is accounted for. And the split is unambiguous:

| Section | ms | What it is |
|---|---|---|
| `Draw.Overlay` | **18.4** | GPU flush-stall = **particle fill** |
| `Present` | 5.7 | buffer swap + geometry raster |
| `Draw.Flush` | 3.6 | `r.End()` GPU flush |
| `Draw.World` | 1.8 | CPU command recording |
| all systems | ~4.5 | (RaycastBullet 2.0 the largest) |

**`Draw.Overlay` 18.4 ms ≈ particle fill.** The correlation is now airtight across every capture: 891 particles → 7.5 ms, 3921 → 18.4 ms, 4003 → 21 ms — a dead-straight ~4.5 µs/particle. It's GPU overdraw, dominated by the flashes, surfacing at the first post-`Draw.World` flush.

**Cell mesh batching worked** — it's just masked. Cells no longer appear as ~1150 draws (`worldDraws` cell contribution → 1), and their fill halved; that cost now lives in the *smaller* `Draw.Flush` + `Present` (~9 ms with 836 cells), not in the 18 ms. The 4168 `worldDraws` that remain are **outlines** (crack/silhouette line segments) + **bullet tracers** — draw *calls*, but thin lines with negligible fill, so they don't gate the frame.

So: **the wall is particle fill, exactly as flagged. Cells and calls are handled; flashes are what's left.**

## The real decision — and it's a feel/perf tradeoff

Here's the honest framing before I touch anything: **at ship settings (`shrapnelCount 260`) the game is already smooth** — ~5× fewer particles → `Draw.Overlay` ~3–4 ms. The 34 ms frame only happens at **1260 shrapnel (5× design)**. So the question is whether to trade normal-play juice for a stress case that won't occur in real play.

The elegant answer that doesn't compromise either: **adaptive emission under load.** Flashes/dust emit at full size/count in normal play; once `ParticleSystem.Count` crosses a soft threshold (approaching a big grenade storm), new flashes shrink and some emissions are skipped. Normal play is untouched; extreme grenades degrade *gracefully* instead of tanking. That matches the "scales forever" philosophy — particle **fill** becomes bounded, just like batching bounded particle **calls**.

The alternatives are a flat flash-size/TTL cut (simpler, but dulls every impact in normal play) or just accepting it (ship settings are fine).

My recommendation: **adaptive under-load emission** — it's the only option that fixes the stress case without dulling normal play. Want me to implement that (a soft particle-budget in `ParticleEffects` that scales flash size/skips emissions as `_fx.Count` climbs), or would you rather accept the stress-case cost and move to Phase 3?

Let's do the adaptive under-load emission. How would you do it? You uniformly skip part of the particles effects once you surpass a certain threshold? Are we sure that the particles are now the real bottleneck?

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-22 14:11 UTC

Good instinct to double-check before optimizing. Let me answer that first, then the design.

**Are we sure it's particles?** Yes — and it's not just the linear correlation, there's effectively a *controlled experiment* already in your log:

| capture | cells | particles | frame | Draw.Overlay |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| 15:05:22 | 1128 | 891 | 19.3 ms | 7.5 ms |
| 15:05:21 | 1047 | **4003** | 36.6 ms | 21 ms |

Cells held ~constant, particles +3100 → frame **+17 ms**. The only variable that moved is particle count, and the frame moved with it. Combined with the mechanism (`Draw.Overlay` contains *zero* particle draw calls, yet tracks particle count → it can only be the GPU flushing `Draw.World`'s pending particle fill), I'm confident. And the adaptive change will self-validate: if it drops the frame, particles were the cause; if not, we were wrong and I'll revert.

**On "uniformly skip" — no, that's the wrong lever, and here's why:** fill ∝ radius², and the fill is dominated by a *few large* particles (flashes at 22–55 px) not the *many small* ones (dust/sparks/trails at ~2 px). Uniformly skipping treats a flash and a trail the same, but skipping one flash saves ~100× the fill of skipping one trail. So uniform skipping would degrade the readable motion (sparks/trails) while barely denting the actual cost.

**Fill-aware degradation instead:**
- Compute a load factor `f = clamp01((Count − soft) / (hard − soft))` (0 below the soft cap → normal play untouched; ramps to 1 at the hard cap).
- **Flashes** (the fill kings): scale their radius down toward a floor *and* skip a growing fraction — `size² ` shrinking is the biggest lever, and flashes overlap so dropping some is invisible.
- **Dust**: reduce the per-burst count under load.
- **Sparks & bullet trails**: leave them — they're cheap and carry the readability.

Let me look at the exact fields, then implement:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-22 14:14 UTC

Adaptive emission is in and building green. What it does:

- **Load factor** ([ParticleEffects.cs](src/Gameplay/ParticleEffects.cs)) — `Load() = clamp01((Count − softCap) / (hardCap − softCap))`. **0 below 1500 live particles**, so normal play is byte-for-byte unchanged; ramps to 1 at 3800.
- **Flashes degrade first** (they're the sole fill kings, since `dustCount` is 0): under load, up to 60% are dropped and survivors shrink toward 35% radius. Because flashes cluster and overlap, and fill is `radius²`, this slashes the dominant cost while staying near-invisible.
- **Sparks and bullet trails untouched** — cheap fill, they carry the readable motion.
- All five knobs are in `game_config.json` → tune live.

**Why this is self-validating:** at ship settings you'll never cross the soft cap, so if the change is sound, normal play looks identical. In your 1260-shrapnel stress case, `Count` climbs past 1500 → flashes thin out → `Draw.Overlay` (the particle-fill line) should drop sharply and the frame recover. If it *doesn't* drop, then particles weren't the cause after all and I'll revert — that's the definitive test you asked for.

Please do one more `Z`/`X` pair:
1. **Normal play** (default 260 shrapnel) — confirm flashes look unchanged and `Draw.Overlay` is small.
2. **1260-shrapnel grenade** — `Draw.Overlay` should fall well below the 18 ms you just saw, and FPS recover. Eyeball whether the reduced flash storm reads acceptably.

If the numbers move as predicted and it looks good, **Phase 2 is done** — grid, particle batching, cell-mesh batching, and now bounded particle fill all scale — and we start **Phase 3** (WinForms→Skia + cross-platform packaging). If `Draw.Overlay` *doesn't* budge, paste it and we'll know particles weren't it.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-22 14:43 UTC

Reverted and building green. Now — **we've found the root cause, and it's definitive.** Here's what your latest captures prove:

## `worldDraws` is now ~330 — so it's NOT draw calls anymore

330 draws can't be 13 ms of call overhead (~0.6 ms at most). Yet `Draw.Overlay`+`Draw.Flush`+`Present` is still ~20 ms. **The bottleneck is now GPU fill rate**, full stop. Draw-call batching is done — grid, particles, cells, tracers, stars all collapsed to a handful of calls.

## And your data *dissociates* the two fill sources — no confound this time

| capture | cells | particles | GPU total |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1461c / **4096p** | 1461 | 4096 | **20.4 ms** |
| **2279c** / 748p | 2279 | 748 | **12.9 ms** |

The second has **more cells but fewer particles** → **less** GPU. If cells were the dominant fill, more cells would mean more time. They don't. So **particle fill dominates**, and it's cleanly separable:

- **Particle fill ≈ 10 ms** at 4096 particles (the flashes) — the biggest reducible cost.
- **Cell mesh fill ≈ 6–7 ms** for 1500–2300 cells — drawn **once** (we removed the union overdraw), culled off-screen → near-minimal. Not reducible without less on-screen asteroid.
- **Present ≈ 4.5 ms** — inherent buffer swap.

## The twist you should know

The adaptive-flash change I just reverted **would actually work now.** Earlier it "did nothing" because draw-call overhead (unbatched tracers/stars) was masking everything at ~20 ms `Draw.Overlay`. Now that calls are batched and `worldDraws` is 330, particle fill is **exposed** as ~10 ms — so reducing flash fill would recover most of it. The earlier test wasn't wrong; it was measuring a different bottleneck.

**So the root cause is settled:** it's GPU fill, and the only *reducible* piece is particle-flash overdraw — which only bites at 4096 particles (5× overload). At ship settings (~800 particles) it's ~2 ms and irrelevant; cells and present are inherent.

Given that, the honest options — and this time we *know* which lever moves it:

This is the breakthrough capture. Everything off — fills (C), particles (V), streaks (B), `streaks 0` confirmed — and **`Draw.Overlay` is STILL 7.6–16.2 ms** with only **157 overlay draws** and 220 `worldDraws`. There's one thing in the overlay I haven't accounted for that does per-frame GPU work independent of scene content — the **border warp** (`IPostEffects.Distort`), which snapshots the framebuffer every frame like the vortex warp we removed. Let me check:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-22 15:26 UTC

**Found it — and it explains everything.** `DrawBorderCue` calls `post.Distort` **up to 4 times per frame** (left/right/top/bottom edges) whenever the player is near a world border and the warp is enabled. Each `Distort` does a `_surface.Snapshot()` — the **exact same per-frame GPU snapshot/sync** that made the vortex warp cost ~4 ms, which we already removed for this reason. Four edge snapshots ≈ 16 ms, and it's in `Draw.Overlay`, **independent of particles/cells/streaks** — a perfect match.

This also re-explains the earlier "dissociation" I misread: `Draw.Overlay` was low (~5.6 ms) in the 2200-cell captures not because of cells, but because **the player wasn't near a border then** (no warp). It was high when near edges. During a grenade the anti-camping mechanic pushes you toward borders → warp active → 12–16 ms.

Let me confirm the config so you can test it instantly:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-22 15:27 UTC

`warpEnabled: true, warpStrength: 7` — it's active. This is a config-only test (no rebuild), so it's the fastest confirmation yet. In [Assets/game_config.json](Assets/game_config.json), in the `borderHazard` block, set:

```json
"warpEnabled": false,
```

Restart the game, then **stand near a world border** (where `Draw.Overlay` was 12–16 ms) and dump.

**The prediction:** `Draw.Overlay` collapses to ~2–5 ms and the frame recovers hard. If it does → the border heat-haze warp is the root cause, full stop: up to **4 GPU framebuffer snapshots per frame**, the identical pattern we killed on the vortex.

This is bigger than the grenade case — it's a **constant 12–16 ms hit any time you're near an edge, in all play**, which is most of a match near the anti-camping border. That's almost certainly the "the game feels heavy" you've been chasing all along.

If it confirms, the fix mirrors what we did for the vortex: either **remove** the border warp (it's a subtle shimmer that isn't worth 4 snapshots) or replace it with a cheap non-snapshot cue (the red depth tint already does the real signaling — the warp is decorative). My recommendation would be to drop it. But confirm the toggle first — paste the near-border capture with `warpEnabled` off, and if it's what I expect, I'll remove it cleanly.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-22 15:31 UTC

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-22 15:32 UTC

Border warp removed, solution green. `IPostEffects.Distort` now has zero callers (both warps gone) — I'll leave the interface for now since it's optional and the WinForms path touches it (Phase 3 cleanup).

**This is a genuine, confirmed win** — the near-border heaviness is gone, in all play, not just grenades.

But your "everything off + warp off" captures reveal the **next floor**, and it's important: with fills/particles/streaks/warp all OFF and only ~170 draws, the frame is *still* 21–24 ms — because **`Draw.Flush` (8 ms) + `Present` (8 ms) ≈ 16 ms of GPU** for a near-empty frame. That's not content anymore; that smells like **resolution or vsync** — a full-screen framebuffer cost independent of what's drawn.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-22 15:33 UTC

Border warp gone, resolution test wired, both build green. Here's where we stand and the two things I need to nail the residual floor.

**Confirmed + fixed:** the border heat-haze warp — 4 GPU snapshots/frame — was `Draw.Overlay`'s 12–16 ms whenever you were near an edge. Gone. That was a real, always-on cost in normal play.

**The remaining floor:** even with everything off, ~16 ms is `Draw.Flush` + `Present` — GPU cost for a near-empty frame, which points at **resolution or forced vsync**, not content.

**Test it empirically** — run windowed at a small resolution:
```bash
cd apps/Game.Sdl && ASTEROIDS_RES=1280x720 dotnet run
```
Then `Z`/`X` in the same heavy scene. **If `Present`+`Draw.Flush` drop from ~16 ms to a few ms at 720p → it's resolution/fill-rate bound** (your display is likely 4K/Retina fullscreen, and the GPU is filling ~8× the pixels). If they *don't* drop, it's forced vsync at the driver/compositor level.

And two quick questions so I can interpret it:
1. **What's your display resolution and refresh rate?** (e.g. 3840×2160 @ 60/120 Hz)
2. **Linux X11 or Wayland?** (Wayland compositors often force vsync regardless of `SwapInterval(0)`.)

Also please grab **one normal-gameplay capture** (all toggles back ON — `V`/`C`/`B` — warp still off) so we see the *real* frame now that the warp's removed — all your recent dumps had content disabled, so they're the floor, not the game.

My strong hunch: you're on a high-DPI display, the 16 ms is fill/present at native res, and at 1080p the whole thing is comfortably 60+. The 720p test will settle it in one run.

Your question is sharp and on the right track. Let me check the vaporization path:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-22 16:05 UTC

Here's the precise answer to your question:

**Vaporization spawns two different things, and only one is batched:**
- **Flash + spark particles** (`ParticleSystem`) → **batched** — one `DrawAtlas` call for all of them.
- **Debris polygon chunks** (`DebrisPiece` from `SpawnDebris`) → **NOT batched** — [`DrawDebris`](src/Gameplay/WorldRenderer.cs) does one `FillPolygon` per chunk. But `worldDraws` stays low (40–301) during your grenades, so debris count isn't large enough to be the ~13 ms grenade cost.

So debris isn't it, and resolution isn't it. That leaves the **4000-particle `DrawAtlas` executing on the GPU** (one call, but 4000 alpha-blended quads — geometry cost, resolution-independent) as the prime remaining suspect. Here's the catch: **every particle test we did was with the warp ON, so the 16 ms warp masked it.** We've never cleanly measured particles with the warp gone.

That's the one clean test left. In a single sustained grenade scene (warp off), dump three times:
1. **Everything ON** (baseline).
2. Press **`V`** (particles off) → dump.
3. Press **`V`** again (back on), press **`C`** (cells off) → dump.

If `Draw.Flush`/frame drops hard on step 2 → it's the particle `DrawAtlas` (fix: bound particle count, or draw them as cheaper flat quads). If step 3 is what moves it → it's the cell mesh. If neither moves → it's genuinely CPU-side (systems ~4 ms + `Draw.World` recording) and we're at the floor.

This time there's no warp to confound it. Paste the three and we'll finally close the book — then Phase 3.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-22 16:29 UTC

This data is clean of the warp, but I hit a gap in my own tooling: **the `X` dump doesn't record which toggles were on/off**, so I can't tell which of these had particles off. What I *can* read is telling, though:

- **`Draw.Flush` (7–14 ms) is resolution-independent** (same at 720p and 1080p) → it's **geometry/vertex processing, not fill**.
- It's **inverse to `worldDraws`** (worldDraws 25 → Flush 13.7; worldDraws 610 → Flush 7.4) → so it's **not the drawn cells/outlines**. It tracks the *particle peak right after detonation* (big fresh flashes), which co-varies with `parts`.

That points back at the particle `DrawAtlas` — but I need it labeled to be sure. Let me fix the dump to record toggle states so the next test is unambiguous:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-22 16:30 UTC

Now every `X` dump self-labels with `[particles:on/OFF fills:on/OFF streaks:on/OFF]`, so the next test can't be ambiguous. **Restart the game** to pick it up.

The one comparison that closes this — in a **sustained grenade** (keep lobbing so `parts` stays ~3800), dump twice:
1. **`X`** (everything on).
2. Press **`V`**, then **`X`** (particles off — the dump will show `particles:OFF`).

If `Draw.Flush` drops from ~10 ms toward ~2 ms with `particles:OFF` → the 3800-sprite `DrawAtlas` is confirmed as the grenade cost (resolution-independent vertex/geometry load), and the fix is simply to **cap particle count** (fewer sprites = fewer quads; at ship-setting 260 shrapnel you'd never approach it anyway). If `Draw.Flush` *doesn't* move → particles are exhausted as a suspect and it's genuinely the GPU pipeline floor, and we stop.

That's the whole test — two labeled dumps, no confound left. Paste them.

For context on where we've landed: **calm play is 75–90 FPS at 1080p** now that the warp's gone — the pervasive "heavy" feel is fixed. This last thread is only about the **5×-overload grenade** (1260 shrapnel); at the shipped 260 it's a non-issue. So whichever way this test goes, we're essentially done with performance — it's just a question of whether there's one more cheap win (particle cap) or we've hit the floor.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-22 16:31 UTC

Now labeled. Let me pull the decisive numbers:

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-22 16:33 UTC

Now it's labeled — and the answer is honest but anticlimactic: **there's no single villain left.** With everything on it's 26.6 ms (38 FPS); toggling each thing off:

| toggle off | frame | Δ |
|---|---|---|
| **particles** (P off) | 23.1 ms | ~3 ms |
| **fills** (F off) | 18.3 ms | ~8 ms |
| **streaks** (S off) | 21.0 ms | ~5 ms |
| **all off** | 12.5 ms | floor |

**Caveat that matters:** these are single captures at *different moments of a transient grenade* (bullet count, particle peak, and cell count all swing second-to-second — notice the two "all off" captures are themselves 12.5 ms and 18.8 ms). So the deltas are rough, not clean A/Bs. But the shape is clear: the grenade cost is **spread across cell fills (~8 ms), streaks (~5 ms), and particles (~3 ms)** — plus bullet raycasts (1–3 ms) — with **no dominant cause**. Every system is individually cheap now. It's resolution-independent, so it's not a fixable GPU-limit; it's just **a lot happening at once at 5× design load.**

## So we've found the root cause — and it's "everything, a little"

The genuine villain was the **border warp** (fixed → 75–90 FPS calm). What remains is diffuse aggregate cost on a **1260-shrapnel (5×) transient**, which doesn't occur at ship settings. Chasing it further is micro-optimization on an extreme case.

Two honest options for the last mile, then I'd stop:

1. **Cap particles** (`ParticleSystem` capacity 4096 → ~2500) — bounds the ~3 ms `DrawAtlas` and the buffer, no visual change at ship settings. Cheap, ~1 edit.
2. **Nothing more** — declare performance done. Grid, particle/cell/tracer/star batching, and the warp removal all scale; the 5× grenade dipping to ~40 FPS for a fraction of a second is acceptable.

My recommendation: **do the particle cap (it's free) and move to Phase 3.** Want me to apply the cap and then start the WinForms→Skia + packaging work? I'll also leave the debug toggles (V/C/B) and `ASTEROIDS_RES` in — they're harmless and useful.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-22 16:37 UTC

## Performance pass — final summary

The one real villain was the **border heat-haze warp** (4 GPU snapshots/frame), which taxed *all* play near the map edges → **calm play went from choppy to 75–90 FPS at 1080p.** Everything else was a supporting cast that we made *scale*:

| Fix | Effect |
|---|---|
| Raycast broad-phase (grid + AABB gates) | `RaycastBulletSystem` 237 ms → ~2 ms |
| Shared `BroadPhaseSystem` + segment query | collision + raycast indexed once/frame |
| Particle / cell / tracer / star batching | `worldDraws` 8300 → ~150 |
| Border warp removed | ~14 ms/frame near edges → gone |
| Particle cap 4096 → 2500 | bounds the `DrawAtlas` load |

Plus a real profiler (`Z`/`X`) with per-system + draw-phase timings, `Present`/`Draw.Flush`/`unaccounted`/draw-call metrics, and debug toggles (`V`/`C`/`B`, `ASTEROIDS_RES`) — all left in, harmless and useful. The 1260-shrapnel (5×) grenade still dips to ~40 FPS for a fraction of a second, but that's diffuse aggregate load at 5× design; at the shipped 260 it's a non-issue.

This is the `net8.0-windows` path, so it won't build on Linux — I'll write it correct-by-construction with a Windows build guide, per our locked decision.

## Phase 3 C2 — cross-platform packaging

- **Publish scripts** ([build/linux.sh](build/linux.sh), [build/macos.sh](build/macos.sh), [build/windows.ps1](build/windows.ps1)) — each does `dotnet publish -c Release -r <rid> --self-contained` and copies `Assets/` beside the exe.
- **Natives verified to bundle** (this I could check from Linux): the Linux publish ships `libSDL2` + `libSkiaSharp` (ran it — complete, runnable), and the `osx-arm64` cross-publish pulls `libSDL2.dylib` + `libSkiaSharp.dylib`. So Linux/macOS are **fully self-contained, no system install needed** — the old "SDL2 isn't bundled" worry was outdated. Windows gets its native via `SkiaSharp.NativeAssets.Win32`.
- **Top-level [README.md](README.md)** — per-OS play/build/publish steps, the native-lib note (with `apt`/`brew` fallback per your choice), and the explicit verification-status caveat.
- `dist/` gitignored; CLAUDE.md build section updated.

## 🧑 User · 2026-07-22 18:25 UTC

How can they run without .NET?

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-22 18:25 UTC

Because `--self-contained` **bundles the entire .NET runtime into the output folder** — there's no separate install because a private copy of .NET ships *inside* the app.

You actually already saw it in the publish output. Those `.so` files next to the game aren't just SDL/Skia:

```
AsteroidsGame            ← 72 KB native launcher ("apphost")
AsteroidsGame.dll        ← your game's IL
libcoreclr.so            ┐
libclrjit.so             │  the .NET 8 runtime (CLR + JIT + GC),
libhostfxr.so            │  copied in by --self-contained
libhostpolicy.so         ┘
System.*.dll             ← the entire base class library (~200 assemblies)
libSDL2-2.0.so           ← native deps
libSkiaSharp.so
Assets/
```

How it runs:
1. The OS launches the small native `AsteroidsGame` executable (the **apphost**).
2. It loads `libhostfxr`/`libhostpolicy` from the *same folder* — not a system-installed .NET — which spin up the bundled `libcoreclr` runtime.
3. That runtime JIT-compiles and runs `AsteroidsGame.dll`, resolving every `System.*.dll` from the folder too.

So the machine needs **nothing pre-installed** — .NET, SDL2, and Skia all live in the folder. That's exactly why it's **85 MB** instead of a couple hundred KB: the ~70 MB runtime + BCL is the price of "no install."

The alternative is a **framework-dependent** publish (drop `--self-contained`): ~1 MB output, but then the user *must* have the matching .NET 8 runtime installed. We chose self-contained for the shippable builds precisely so end users never touch a .NET installer — while `dotnet run` during development still uses your installed SDK.

One caveat worth knowing: self-contained is **per-OS/architecture** — the `linux-x64` folder only runs on 64-bit Linux, `osx-arm64` only on Apple-Silicon Macs, etc. That's why there are three separate build scripts, one per RID.

## 🧑 User · 2026-07-22 18:33 UTC

So I have to build the game for every RID (including the .NET runtime) (70MB per RID) and ship the executable directly with no dependencies?

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-22 18:34 UTC

Almost exactly right — with **one correction**: you ship the **folder**, not just the executable. The `AsteroidsGame` launcher alone won't run; it needs its sibling `.dll`s, the native `.so`/`.dll`/`.dylib` files, and `Assets/` right beside it. So:

- **One self-contained build per RID** you want to support — each ~85 MB, each including its own copy of the .NET runtime.
- **Distribute the whole `dist/<rid>/` folder** (usually zipped). End user unzips and runs the launcher. No .NET, no SDL, no Skia to install.

RIDs you'd realistically ship: `win-x64`, `linux-x64`, `osx-arm64` (+ `osx-x64` for Intel Macs and maybe `win-arm64` if you care). So 3–5 builds.

## If you want it to be a *single file* instead of a folder

That's the more shippable form, and it's one MSBuild flag:

| Option | Flag | Effect |
|---|---|---|
| **Single-file** | `PublishSingleFile=true` | Bundles all the DLLs (and can embed the natives) into **one** executable. Still ~85 MB, but literally one file to hand someone. Assets stay external. |
| **Trimming** | `PublishTrimmed=true` | Strips unused BCL — can cut the ~70 MB runtime down a lot. **Risky**: it can break reflection/JSON deserialization (your config loader), so it needs testing per-platform. |
| **ReadyToRun** | `PublishReadyToRun=true` | AOT-precompiles for faster startup; *larger* size. |

My recommendation for shipping: **single-file, no trimming** — one clean executable per RID, and no risk of trimming silently breaking `GameConfigLoader`'s JSON reflection. `Assets/` still ships alongside (it's data you want editable/visible anyway).

The **framework-dependent** alternative (drop `--self-contained`) is ~1–2 MB and cross-RID for the same OS, but then every user must install the .NET 8 runtime first — worse for a game you're handing to players.

Want me to switch the build scripts to **single-file** self-contained (add `-p:PublishSingleFile=true -p:IncludeNativeLibrariesForSelfExtract=true`)? That gives you `dist/<rid>/AsteroidsGame` (one exe) + `Assets/`, which is the cleanest thing to zip and share.

## 🤖 Claude · 2026-07-22 19:05 UTC

That's the real thing. Here's the full picture for all three RIDs and your two questions.

## What each directory looks like

```
dist/linux-x64/           (85 MB, ~208 files)
├── AsteroidsGame              ← the launcher you run (71 KB native apphost)
├── AsteroidsGame.dll          ← your game code
├── libSDL2-2.0.so             ← windowing/input (bundled)
├── libSkiaSharp.so            ← GPU renderer (bundled, 9 MB)
├── System.*.dll  ×~200        ← the entire .NET 8 runtime + BCL
├── AsteroidsGame.deps.json    ← runtime config (leave it)
├── AsteroidsGame.runtimeconfig.json
└── Assets/                    ← game_config.json + shapes/*.json
```

The other two are identical in shape, just different launcher + native extensions:

| RID | Launcher to run | Natives |
|---|---|---|
| `linux-x64` | `AsteroidsGame` | `libSDL2-2.0.so`, `libSkiaSharp.so` |
| `osx-arm64` | `AsteroidsGame` | `libSDL2-2.0.dylib`, `libSkiaSharp.dylib` |
| `win-x64` | `AsteroidsGame.WinForms.exe` | `SDL2.dll`?(no — WinForms), `libSkiaSharp.dll` |

*(Windows is the WinForms build, so no SDL — just `libSkiaSharp.dll`.)*

**Users don't touch any of the ~200 files** — they run one launcher; everything else is plumbing beside it.

## Publishing to GitHub — use Releases, not the repo

**Don't commit the folders into git** — that's 255 MB of binaries bloating every clone forever, and git isn't built for it (that's why I gitignored `dist/`). The standard path:

1. **Zip each folder:** `AsteroidsGame-linux-x64.zip`, `AsteroidsGame-win-x64.zip`, `AsteroidsGame-osx-arm64.zip` (each ~40–50 MB compressed).
2. On GitHub → **Releases** → *Draft a new release* → tag it (e.g. `v1.0`) → **drag the three zips in as assets**.
3. Users go to the Releases page and download **only their OS's zip** — GitHub shows them as a simple download list. The repo stays lean; the binaries live as release attachments.

*(This can later be automated with a GitHub Actions workflow that runs the three publish scripts on Windows/Linux/macOS runners and uploads the zips on every tag — but manual upload is fine to start.)*

## How a user runs it after downloading

**Windows** — unzip, double-click `AsteroidsGame.WinForms.exe`.
- Gotcha: SmartScreen will warn "unknown publisher" (the exe is unsigned) → *More info → Run anyway*. Code-signing removes this but costs money; fine to skip for now.

**Linux** — unzip, then:
```bash
chmod +x AsteroidsGame     # zip usually drops the executable bit
./AsteroidsGame
```
- Gotcha: needs a couple of ubiquitous system libs present (`libGL`, `libfontconfig`) — on any desktop Linux they already are.

**macOS** — unzip, then:
```bash
xattr -dr com.apple.quarantine AsteroidsGame*   # clear Gatekeeper's "downloaded from internet" flag
chmod +x AsteroidsGame
./AsteroidsGame
```
- Gotcha: Gatekeeper blocks unsigned downloaded apps hard. The `xattr` line (or right-click → Open the first time) is required; notarizing/signing avoids it but needs an Apple Developer account.

The **only** hard requirement in all three cases is keeping `Assets/` next to the launcher — which the zip preserves automatically.

Want me to add a **`build/package.sh`** that runs a publish and zips the result to `dist/AsteroidsGame-<rid>.zip` (ready to drag into a GitHub release), and/or drop a short "How to run" `.txt` into each published folder so users see the chmod/Gatekeeper steps?

## What you now have in `build/`

| Script | Purpose | Output |
|---|---|---|
| `linux.sh` / `macos.sh` | quick single runnable folder (dev/testing) | `dist/<rid>/` |
| **`package.sh`** | **publish + zip + `RUN.txt`** for release | `dist/AsteroidsGame-<rid>.zip` |
| `windows.ps1` | Windows equivalent of package.sh (WinForms build) | `dist/AsteroidsGame-win-x64.zip` |

**`package.sh`** cross-publishes the Unix RIDs from your Linux box (`linux-x64`, `osx-arm64` by default, or pass RIDs). Each zip unpacks to a self-descriptive `AsteroidsGame-<rid>/` folder containing the launcher, bundled runtime + natives, `Assets/`, and a **`RUN.txt`** with the exact per-OS launch steps (the `chmod` / Gatekeeper `xattr` / SmartScreen gotchas). `windows.ps1` produces the matching `win-x64` zip — run it on Windows, since the WinForms build can't be made on Linux.

**The release flow:**
```bash
build/package.sh          # → dist/AsteroidsGame-linux-x64.zip + AsteroidsGame-osx-arm64.zip
# on a Windows machine:
build/windows.ps1         # → dist/AsteroidsGame-win-x64.zip
```
Then GitHub → **Releases** → *Draft new release* → tag `v1.0` → drag the three zips in as assets. Users download only their platform's zip, unzip, and follow `RUN.txt`.

I verified (from Linux) that the `linux-x64` zip is a complete, self-contained 37 MB archive with the launcher, both native libs, and the full `Assets/`. The `osx-arm64` path pulls the correct `.dylib` natives; `win-x64` is correct-by-construction and needs a run on Windows to confirm.
